          <a href="#cite_note-8">

          </a>
         </sup>

      <p>
       <b>
        Chiapas
       </b>
       (
       <small>
        Spanish pronunciation:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">
         [ˈtʃjapas]
        </a>
       </span>
       <small class="nowrap">
        (
        <span class="unicode haudio">
         <span class="fn">
          <span style="white-space:nowrap">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Chiapas1.ogg" title="About this sound">
            <img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/>
           </a>
          </span>
          <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cf/Chiapas1.ogg" title="Chiapas1.ogg">
           listen
          </a>
         </span>
        </span>
        )
       </small>
       ), officially the
       <b>
        Free and Sovereign State of Chiapas
       </b>
       (
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">
        Spanish
       </a>
       :
       <i>
        <span lang="es" xml:lang="es">
         Estado Libre y Soberano de Chiapas
        </span>
       </i>
       ), is one of the 31 states that with
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">
        Mexico City
       </a>
       make up the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_divisions_of_Mexico" title="Political divisions of Mexico">
        32 federal entities
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">
        Mexico
       </a>
       . It is divided into
       <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Chiapas" title="Municipalities of Chiapas">
        124 municipalities
       </a>
       as of September 2017
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and its capital city is
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
        Tuxtla Gutiérrez
       </a>
       . Other important population centers in Chiapas include
       <a href="/wiki/Ocosingo" title="Ocosingo">
        Ocosingo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tapachula" title="Tapachula">
        Tapachula
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_las_Casas" title="San Cristóbal de las Casas">
        San Cristóbal de las Casas
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Comit%C3%A1n" title="Comitán">
        Comitán
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arriaga,_Chiapas" title="Arriaga, Chiapas">
        Arriaga
       </a>
       . It is the southernmost state, bordered by the states of
       <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
        Oaxaca
       </a>
       to the west,
       <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">
        Veracruz
       </a>
       to the northwest and
       <a href="/wiki/Tabasco" title="Tabasco">
        Tabasco
       </a>
       to the north,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EB1911-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and by the
       <a href="/wiki/Pet%C3%A9n_Department" title="Petén Department">
        Petén
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Quich%C3%A9_Department" title="Quiché Department">
        Quiché
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Huehuetenango_Department" title="Huehuetenango Department">
        Huehuetenango
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/San_Marcos_Department" title="San Marcos Department">
        San Marcos
       </a>
       departments of
       <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">
        Guatemala
       </a>
       to the east and southeast. Chiapas has a coastline along the
       <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">
        Pacific Ocean
       </a>
       to the south.
      </p>
      <p>
       In general, Chiapas has a humid, tropical climate. In the north, in the area bordering Tabasco, near
       <a href="/wiki/Teapa_Municipality" title="Teapa Municipality">
        Teapa
       </a>
       , rainfall can average more than 3,000 mm (120 in) per year. In the past, natural vegetation in this region was lowland, tall perennial
       <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">
        rainforest
       </a>
       , but this vegetation has been almost completely cleared to allow agriculture and ranching. Rainfall decreases moving towards the
       <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">
        Pacific Ocean
       </a>
       , but it is still abundant enough to allow the farming of bananas and many other tropical crops near
       <a href="/wiki/Tapachula" title="Tapachula">
        Tapachula
       </a>
       . On the several parallel "sierras" or mountain ranges running along the center of Chiapas, climate can be quite temperate and foggy, allowing the development of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cloud_forests" title="Cloud forests">
        cloud forests
       </a>
       like those of the Reserva de la Biosfera el Triunfo, home to a handful of
       <a href="/wiki/Resplendent_quetzal" title="Resplendent quetzal">
        resplendent quetzals
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Horned_guan" title="Horned guan">
        horned guans
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Chiapas is home to the ancient
       <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">
        Mayan
       </a>
       ruins of
       <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">
        Palenque
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaxchil%C3%A1n" title="Yaxchilán">
        Yaxchilán
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bonampak" title="Bonampak">
        Bonampak
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chinkultic" title="Chinkultic">
        Chinkultic
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tonin%C3%A1" title="Toniná">
        Toniná
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is also home to one of the largest indigenous populations in the country with twelve federally recognized ethnicities. Much of the state’s history is centered on the subjugation of these peoples with occasional rebellions. The last of these rebellions was the
       <a href="/wiki/Zapatista_Army_of_National_Liberation" title="Zapatista Army of National Liberation">
        1994 Zapatista uprising
       </a>
       , which succeeded in obtaining new rights for indigenous people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs a reliable source; the laws produced as a result of the Zapatista uprising are arguably not being enforced. (January 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The official name of the state is Chiapas. It is believed to have come from the ancient city of Chiapan, which in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C3%A1huatl" title="Náhuatl">
        Náhuatl
       </a>
       means "the place where the
       <a href="/wiki/Salvia_hispanica" title="Salvia hispanica">
        chia sage
       </a>
       grows."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After the Spanish arrived (1522), they established two cities called Chiapas de
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       los Indios and Chiapas de
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       los Españoles (1528), with the name of Provincia de Chiapas for the area around the cities. The first coat of arms of the region dates from 1535 as that of the Ciudad Real (
       <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_las_Casas" title="San Cristóbal de las Casas">
        San Cristóbal de las Casas
       </a>
       ). Chiapas painter
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Javier_Vargas_Ballinas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Javier Vargas Ballinas (page does not exist)">
        Javier Vargas Ballinas
       </a>
       designed the modern coat of arms.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nomenclatura_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-nomenclatura-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
          <span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (November 2016)">
           need quotation to verify
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Columbian_Era">
        Pre-Columbian Era
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Columbian Era">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3272" data-file-width="2443" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG/150px-JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG/225px-JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG/300px-JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG 2x" width="150"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JaguarStoneMusRegTuxtla.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Jaguar sculpture from Cintalapa dating between 1000 and 400
         <span class="nowrap">
         </span>
         BCE on display at the
         <a href="/wiki/Regional_Museum_of_Anthropology_and_History_of_Chiapas" title="Regional Museum of Anthropology and History of Chiapas">
          Regional Museum of Anthropology and History of Chiapas
         </a>
         .
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Hunter gatherers began to occupy the central valley of the state around 7000 BCE, but little is known about them. The oldest archaeological remains in the seat are located at the Santa Elena Ranch in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocozocoautla" title="Ocozocoautla">
        Ocozocoautla
       </a>
       whose finds include tools and weapons made of stone and bone. It also includes burials.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones29_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones29-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the
       <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">
        pre Classic period
       </a>
       from 1800 BCE to 300 CE, agricultural villages appeared all over the state although hunter gather groups would persist for long after the era.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Recent excavations in the
       <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">
        Soconusco
       </a>
       region of the state indicate that the oldest civilization to appear in what is now modern Chiapas is that of the
       <a href="/wiki/Mokaya" title="Mokaya">
        Mokaya
       </a>
       , which were cultivating corn and living in houses as early as 1500 BCE, making them one of the oldest in Mesoamerica.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones35_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones35-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is speculation that these were the forefathers of the
       <a href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">
        Olmec
       </a>
       , migrating across the Grijalva Valley and onto the coastal plain of the Gulf of Mexico to the north, which was Olmec territory. One of these people's ancient cities is now the archeological site of
       <a href="/wiki/Chiapa_de_Corzo_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Chiapa de Corzo (Mesoamerican site)">
        Chiapa de Corzo
       </a>
       , in which was found the oldest calendar known on a piece of ceramic with a date of 36 BCE. This is three hundred years before the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayan_peoples" title="Mayan peoples">
        Mayans
       </a>
       developed their calendar. The descendants of Mokaya are the Mixe-Zoque.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the pre Classic era, it is known that most of Chiapas was not Olmec, but had close relations with them, especially the Olmecs of the Isthmus of
       <a href="/wiki/Tehuantepec" title="Tehuantepec">
        Tehuantepec
       </a>
       . Olmec-influenced sculpture can be found in Chiapas and products from the state including
       <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">
        amber
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetite" title="Magnetite">
        magnetite
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ilmenite" title="Ilmenite">
        ilmenite
       </a>
       were exported to Olmec lands. The Olmecs came to what is now the northwest of the state looking for amber with one of the main pieces of evidence for this called the Simojovel Ax.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-olmecasarq_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-olmecasarq-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palace_at_Palenque.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="518" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Palace_at_Palenque.jpg/220px-Palace_at_Palenque.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Palace_at_Palenque.jpg/330px-Palace_at_Palenque.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Palace_at_Palenque.jpg/440px-Palace_at_Palenque.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palace_at_Palenque.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Palace at
         <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">
          Palenque
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Mayan civilization began in the pre-Classic period as well, but did not come into prominence until the
       <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">
        Classic period
       </a>
       (300–900
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       CE). Development of this culture was agricultural villages during the pre-Classic period with city building during the Classic as social stratification became more complex. The Mayans built cities on the
       <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">
        Yucatán Peninsula
       </a>
       and west into
       <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">
        Guatemala
       </a>
       . In Chiapas, Mayan sites are concentrated along the state's borders with
       <a href="/wiki/Tabasco" title="Tabasco">
        Tabasco
       </a>
       and Guatemala, near Mayan sites in those entities. Most of this area belongs to the
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_Jungle" title="Lacandon Jungle">
        Lacandon Jungle
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones2930_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones2930-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mayan civilization in the Lacandon area is marked by rising exploitation of rain forest resources, rigid social stratification, fervent local identity, waging war against neighboring peoples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At its height, it had large cities, a writing system, and development of scientific knowledge, such as mathematics and astronomy. Cities were centered on large political and ceremonial structures elaborately decorated with murals and inscriptions. Among these cities are
       <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">
        Palenque
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bonampak" title="Bonampak">
        Bonampak
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yaxchilan" title="Yaxchilan">
        Yaxchilan
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chinkultic" title="Chinkultic">
        Chinkultic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tonin%C3%A1" title="Toniná">
        Toniná
       </a>
       and Tenón. The Mayan civilization had extensive trade networks and large markets trading in goods such as animal skins,
       <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">
        indigo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">
        amber
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">
        vanilla
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Quetzal" title="Quetzal">
        quetzal
       </a>
       feathers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones2930_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones2930-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is not known what ended the civilization but theories range from over population size, natural disasters, disease, and loss of natural resources through over exploitation or climate change.
      </p>
      <p>
       Nearly all Mayan cities collapsed around the same time, 900
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       CE. From then
       <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">
        until 1500 CE
       </a>
       , social organization of the region fragmented into much smaller units and social structure became much less complex. There was some influence from the rising powers of central Mexico but two main indigenous groups emerged during this time, the Zoques and the various Mayan descendents. The Chiapans, for whom the state is named, migrated into the center of the state during this time and settled around Chiapa de Corzo, the old Mixe–Zoque stronghold.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There is evidence that the
       <a href="/wiki/Aztec" title="Aztec">
        Aztecs
       </a>
       appeared in the center of the state around Chiapa de Corza in the 15th
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       century, but were unable to displace the native Chiapa tribe. However, they had enough influence so that the name of this area and of the state would come from
       <a href="/wiki/Nahuatl" title="Nahuatl">
        Nahuatl
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Colonial_period">
        Colonial period
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Colonial period">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_Chiapas" title="Spanish conquest of Chiapas">
        Spanish conquest of Chiapas
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg/220px-ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg/330px-ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg/440px-ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChiapadeCorzoFuenteColonial.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Royal Crown centered in the main plaza of
         <a href="/wiki/Chiapa_de_Corzo,_Chiapas" title="Chiapa de Corzo, Chiapas">
          Chiapa de Corzo
         </a>
         built in 1562.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       When the Spanish arrived in the 16th century, they found the indigenous peoples divided into Mayan and non-Mayan, with the latter dominated by the
       <a href="/wiki/Zoque_people" title="Zoque people">
        Zoques
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapa_people&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chiapa people (page does not exist)">
        Chiapa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first contact between Spaniards and the people of Chiapas came in 1522, when
       <a href="/wiki/Hern%C3%A1n_Cort%C3%A9s" title="Hernán Cortés">
        Hernán Cortés
       </a>
       sent tax collectors to the area after Aztec Empire was subdued. The first military incursion was headed by Luis Marín, who arrived in 1523. For three years, Marín was able to subjugate a number of the local peoples, but met with fierce resistance from the
       <a href="/wiki/Tzotzil_people" title="Tzotzil people">
        Tzotzils
       </a>
       in the highlands. The Spanish colonial government then sent a new expedition under
       <a href="/wiki/Diego_de_Mazariegos" title="Diego de Mazariegos">
        Diego de Mazariegos
       </a>
       . Mazariegos had more success than his predecessor, but many indigenes preferred to commit suicide rather than submit to the Spanish. One famous example of this is the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Battle_of_Tepetchia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Battle of Tepetchia (page does not exist)">
        Battle of Tepetchia
       </a>
       , where many jumped to their deaths in the
       <a href="/wiki/Sumidero_Canyon" title="Sumidero Canyon">
        Sumidero Canyon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Indigenous resistance was weakened by continual warfare with the Spaniards as well as disease, and by 1530, almost all of the indigenous peoples of the area had been subdued with the exception of the
       <a href="/wiki/Lakandon_Ch%27ol" title="Lakandon Ch'ol">
        Lacandons
       </a>
       in the deep jungles who actively resisted until 1695.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, the main two groups, the Tzotzils and
       <a href="/wiki/Tzeltal_people" title="Tzeltal people">
        Tzeltals
       </a>
       of the central highlands were subdued enough to establish the first Spanish city, today called
       <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_las_Casas" title="San Cristóbal de las Casas">
        San Cristóbal de las Casas
       </a>
       , in 1528. It was one of two settlements initially called Villa Real de Chiapa de los Españoles and the other called Chiapa de los Indios.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG/250px-RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG/375px-RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG/500px-RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RemnantPaintCupolaSanMarcosTuxtla.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Remnants of frescos at the Saint Mark Cathedral of
         <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
          Tuxtla Gutiérrez
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Soon after, the
       <a href="/wiki/Encomienda" title="Encomienda">
        encomienda
       </a>
       system was introduced, which reduced most of the indigenous population to serfs and many even as slaves, paid as a form of tribute. The
       <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">
        conquistadors
       </a>
       brought previously unknown diseases. This, as well as overwork on plantations, dramatically decreased the indigenous population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Spanish also established missions, mostly under the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">
        Dominicans
       </a>
       , with the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_Las_Casas" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of San Cristóbal de Las Casas">
        Diocese of Chiapas
       </a>
       established in 1538 by
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">
        Pope Paul III
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Dominican evangelizers became early advocates of the indigenous' plight, with
       <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">
        Bartolomé de las Casas
       </a>
       winning a battle with the passing of a law in 1542 for their protection. This order also worked to make sure that communities would keep their indigenous name with a saint’s prefix leading to names such as
       <a href="/wiki/Chamula" title="Chamula">
        San Juan Chamula
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Zinacant%C3%A1n" title="Zinacantán">
        San Lorenzo Zinacantán
       </a>
       . He also advocated adapting the teaching of Christianity to indigenous language and culture. The encomienda system that had perpetrated much of the abuse of the indigenous peoples fell away by the end of the 16th century, and was replaced by
       <a href="/wiki/Hacienda" title="Hacienda">
        haciendas
       </a>
       . However, the use and misuse of Indian labor remained a large part of Chiapas politics into modern times.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This treatment and tribute payments would create an undercurrent of resentment in the indigenous population that passed on from generation to generation. One uprising against high tribute payments occurs in the Tzeltal communities in the Los Alto region in 1712. Soon, the Tzoltzils and Ch'ols joined the Tzeltales in rebellion, but within a year, the government was able to extinguish the rebellion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As of 1778,
       <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Kitchin" title="Thomas Kitchin">
        Thomas Kitchin
       </a>
       described Chiapas as "the metropolis of the original Mexicans," with a population of approximately 20,000, and consisting mainly of indigenous peoples.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kitchin_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kitchin-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Spanish introduced new crops such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">
        sugar cane
       </a>
       , wheat,
       <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">
        barley
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Indigo" title="Indigo">
        indigo
       </a>
       as main economic staples along native ones such as corn, cotton,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cacao_bean" title="Cacao bean">
        cacao
       </a>
       and beans. Livestock such as cattle, horses and sheep were introduced as well. Regions would specialize in certain crops and animals depending on local conditions and for many of these regions, communication and travel were difficult.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most Europeans and their descendents tended to concentrate in cities such as
       <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_las_Casas" title="San Cristóbal de las Casas">
        Ciudad Real
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Comit%C3%A1n" title="Comitán">
        Comitán
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chiapa_de_Corzo,_Chiapas" title="Chiapa de Corzo, Chiapas">
        Chiapa
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
        Tuxtla
       </a>
       . Intermixing of the races was prohibited by colonial law but by the end of the 17th century there was a significant
       <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">
        mestizo
       </a>
       population. Added to this was a population of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Mexican" title="Afro-Mexican">
        African slaves brought in by the Spanish
       </a>
       in the middle of the 16th century due to the loss of native workforce.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones3031_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones3031-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Initially, "Chiapas" referred to the first two cities established by the Spanish in what is now the center of the state and the area surrounding them. Two other regions were also established, the
       <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">
        Soconusco
       </a>
       and Tuxtla, all under the
       <a href="/wiki/Captaincy_General_of_Guatemala" title="Captaincy General of Guatemala">
        regional colonial government of Guatemala
       </a>
       . Chiapas, Soconusco and Tuxla regions were united to the first time as an
       <i>
        intendencia
       </i>
       during the
       <a href="/wiki/Bourbon_Reforms" title="Bourbon Reforms">
        Bourbon Reforms
       </a>
       in 1790 as an administrative region under the name of Chiapas. However, within this intendencia, the division between Chiapas and Soconusco regions would remain strong and have consequences at the end of the colonial period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones35_19-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones35-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Era_of_Independence">
        Era of Independence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Era of Independence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Since the colonial period, Chiapas had been relatively isolated from colonial authorities in Mexico City and regional authorities in Guatemala. One reason for this was the rugged terrain but the other was that much of Chiapas was not attractive to the Spanish for its lack of mineral wealth, large areas of arable land, and easy access to markets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This isolation spared it from battles related to Independence.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Morelos_y_Pav%C3%B3n" title="José María Morelos y Pavón">
        José María Morelos y Pavón
       </a>
       did enter the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Tonal%C3%A1,_Chiapas" title="Tonalá, Chiapas">
        Tonalá
       </a>
       but incurred no resistance. The only other insurgent activity was the publication of a newspaper called
       <i>
        El Pararrayos
       </i>
       by Matías de Córdova in San Cristóbal de las Casas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones31_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones31-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ComitanDecIndependence.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/ComitanDecIndependence.jpg/220px-ComitanDecIndependence.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/ComitanDecIndependence.jpg/330px-ComitanDecIndependence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/ComitanDecIndependence.jpg/440px-ComitanDecIndependence.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ComitanDecIndependence.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Comitán's declaration of independence from 1823
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2588" data-file-width="2592" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg/220px-StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg/330px-StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg/440px-StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:StateConstitution1824Tuxtla.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Copy of the 1825 state constitution
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2076" data-file-width="2592" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg/220px-1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg/330px-1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg/440px-1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1856MapChiapasTuxtla.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         1856 map of the state
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Following the end of Spanish rule in
       <a href="/wiki/New_Spain" title="New Spain">
        New Spain
       </a>
       , it was unclear what new political arrangements would emerge. The isolation of Chiapas from centers of power, along with the strong internal divisions in the intendencia caused a political crisis after royal government collapsed in Mexico City in 1821, ending the
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_War_of_Independence" title="Mexican War of Independence">
        Mexican War of Independence
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During this war, a group of influential Chiapas merchants and ranchers sought the establishment of the Free State of Chiapas. This group became known as the
       <i>
        La Familia Chiapaneca
       </i>
       . However, this alliance did not last with the lowlands preferring inclusion among the new republics of
       <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">
        Central America
       </a>
       and the highlands annexation to Mexico.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens81_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens81-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1821, a number of cities in Chiapas, starting in Comitán, declared the state's separation from the Spanish empire. In 1823, Guatemala became part of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Provinces_of_Central_America" title="United Provinces of Central America">
        United Provinces of Central America
       </a>
       , which united to form a federal republic that would last from 1823 to 1839. With the exception of the pro-Mexican Ciudad Real (San Cristóbal) and some others, many Chiapanecan towns and villages favored a Chiapas independent of Mexico and some favored unification with Guatemala.
      </p>
      <p>
       Elites in highland cities pushed for incorporation into Mexico.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1822, then Emperor
       <a href="/wiki/Agust%C3%ADn_de_Iturbide" title="Agustín de Iturbide">
        Agustín de Iturbide
       </a>
       decreed that Chiapas was part of Mexico. In 1823, the Junta General de Gobierno was held and Chiapas declared independence again.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In July 1824, the Soconusco District of southwestern Chiapas split off from Chiapas, announcing that it would join the Central American Federation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In September of the same year, a referendum was held on whether the intendencia would join Central America or Mexico, with many of the elite endorsing union with Mexico. This referendum ended in favor of incorporation with Mexico (allegedly through manipulation of the elite in the highlands), but the
       <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">
        Soconusco
       </a>
       region maintained a neutral status until 1842, when Oaxacans under General
       <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">
        Antonio López de Santa Anna
       </a>
       occupied the area, and declared it reincorporated into Mexico. Elites of the area would not accept this until 1844.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens81_28-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens81-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones32_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones32-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Guatemala would not recognize Mexico's annexation of the Soconusco region until 1895 even though a final border between Chiapas and the country was finalized until 1882.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones32_29-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones32-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The State of Chiapas was officially declared in 1824, with its first constitution in 1826. Ciudad Real was renamed San Cristóbal de las Casas in 1828.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones35_19-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones35-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the decades after the official end of the war, the provinces of Chiapas and Soconusco unified, with power concentrated in San Cristóbal de las Casas. The state's society evolved into three distinct spheres: indigenous peoples, mestizos from the farms and haciendas and the Spanish colonial cities. Most of the political struggles were between the latter two groups especially over who would control the indigenous labor force. Economically, the state lost one of its main crops, indigo, to synthetic dyes. There was a small experiment with democracy in the form of "open city councils" but it was short lived because voting was heavily rigged.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Universidad Pontificia y Literaria de Chiapas was founded in 1826, with Mexico's second teacher’s college founded in the state in 1828.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Era_of_the_Liberal_Reform">
        Era of the Liberal Reform
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Era of the Liberal Reform">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       With the ouster of conservative
       <a href="/wiki/Antonio_L%C3%B3pez_de_Santa_Anna" title="Antonio López de Santa Anna">
        Antonio López de Santa Anna
       </a>
       , Mexican liberals came to power. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/War_of_the_Reform" title="War of the Reform">
        Reform War
       </a>
       (1858–1861) fought between Liberals, who favored federalism and sought economic development, decreased power of the Roman Catholic Church, and Mexican army, and Conservatives, who favored centralized autocratic government, retention of elite privileges, did not lead to any military battles in the state. Despite that it strongly affected Chiapas politics.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Chiapas, the Liberal-Conservative division had its own twist. Much of the division between the highland and lowland ruling families was for whom the Indians should work for and for how long as the main shortage was of labor.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens84_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens84-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These families split into Liberals in the lowlands, who wanted further reform and Conservatives in the highlands who still wanted to keep some of the traditional colonial and church privileges.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens85_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens85-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For most of the early and mid 19th century, Conservatives held most of the power and were concentrated in the larger cities of San Cristóbal de las Casas, Chiapa (de Corzo), Tuxtla and Comitán. As Liberals gained the upper hand nationally in the mid-19th century, one Liberal politician Ángel Albino Corzo gained control of the state. Corzo became the primary exponent of Liberal ideas in the southeast of Mexico and defended the Palenque and
       <a href="/wiki/Pichucalco" title="Pichucalco">
        Pichucalco
       </a>
       areas from annexation by Tabasco. However, Corzo's rule would end in 1875, when he opposed the regime of
       <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">
        Porfirio Díaz
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Liberal land reforms would have negative effects on the state's indigenous population unlike in other areas of the country. Liberal governments expropriated lands that were previously held by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_Crown" title="Spanish Crown">
        Spanish Crown
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
        Catholic Church
       </a>
       in order to sell them into private hands. This was not only motivated by ideology, but also due to the need to raise money. However, many of these lands had been in a kind of "trust" with the local indigenous populations, who worked them. Liberal reforms took away this arrangement and many of these lands fell into the hands of large landholders who when made the local Indian population work for three to five days a week just for the right to continue to cultivate the lands. This requirement caused many to leave and look for employment elsewhere. Most became "free" workers on other farms, but they were often paid only with food and basic necessities from the farm shop. If this was not enough, these workers became indebted to these same shops and then unable to leave.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens8283_32-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens8283-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The opening up of these lands also allowed many whites and mestizos (often called Ladinos in Chiapas) to encroach on what had been exclusively indigenous communities in the state. These communities had had almost no contact with the Ladino world, except for a priest. The new Ladino landowners occupied their acquired lands as well as others, such as shopkeepers, opened up businesses in the center of Indian communities. In 1848, a group of
       <a href="/wiki/Tzeltal_people" title="Tzeltal people">
        Tzeltals
       </a>
       plotted to kill the new mestizos in their midst, but this plan was discovered, and was punished by th removal of large number of the community’s male members. The changing social order had severe negative effects on the indigenous population with alcoholism spreadings, leading to more debts as it was expensive.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens84_30-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens84-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The struggles between Conservatives and Liberals nationally disrupted commerce and confused power relations between Indian communities and Ladino authorities. It also resulted in some brief respites for Indians during times when the instability led to uncollected taxes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens86_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens86-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       One other effect that Liberal land reforms had was the start of coffee plantations, especially in the
       <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">
        Soconusco
       </a>
       region. One reason for this push in this area was that Mexico was still working to strengthen its claim on the area against Guatemala’s claims on the region. The land reforms brought colonists from other areas of the country as well as foreigners from England, the United States and France. These foreign immigrants would introduce coffee production to the areas, as well as modern machineray and professional administration of coffee plantations. Eventually, this production of coffee would become the state's most important crop.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martineztorres_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-martineztorres-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although the Liberals had mostly triumphed in the state and the rest of the country by the 1860s, Conservatives still held considerable power in Chiapas. Liberal politicians sought to solidify their power among the indigenous groups by weakening the Roman Catholic Church. The more radical of these even allowed indigenous groups the religious freedoms to return to a number of native rituals and beliefs such as pilgrimages to natural shrines such as mountains and waterfalls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens8788_35-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens8788-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This culminated in the Chiapas "caste war", which was an uprising the
       <a href="/wiki/Tzotzil_people" title="Tzotzil people">
        Tzotzils
       </a>
       beginning in 1868.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens88_36-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens88-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The basis of the uprising was the establishment of the "three stones cult" in Tzajahemal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens88_36-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens88-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Agustina_G%C3%B3mez_Checheb&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Agustina Gómez Checheb (page does not exist)">
        Agustina Gómez Checheb
       </a>
       was a girl tending her father’s sheep when three stones fell from the sky. Collecting them, she put them on her father’s altar and soon claimed that the stone communicated with her. Word of this soon spread and the "talking stones" of Tzajahemel soon became a local indigenous pilgrimage site. The cult was taken over by one pilgrim,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pedro_D%C3%ADaz_Cuzcat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pedro Díaz Cuzcat (page does not exist)">
        Pedro Díaz Cuzcat
       </a>
       , who also claimed to be able to communicate with the stones, and had knowledge of Catholic ritual, becoming a kind of priest. However, this challenged the traditional Catholic faith and non Indians began to denounce the cult.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens80_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens80-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Stories about the cult include embellishments such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Crucifixion" title="Crucifixion">
        crucifixion
       </a>
       of a young Indian boy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens81_28-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens81-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       This led to the arrest of Checheb and Cuzcat in December 1868. This caused resentment among the Tzotzils. Although the Liberals had earlier supported the cult, Liberal landowners had also lost control of much of their Indian labor and Liberal politicians were having a harder time collecting taxes from indigenous communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens8990_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens8990-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An Indian army gathered at Zontehuitz then attacked various villages and haciendas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones32_29-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones32-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the following June the city of San Cristóbal was surrounded by several thousand Indians, who offered the exchanged of several Ladino captives for their religious leaders and stones.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens90_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens90-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Chiapas governor Dominguéz come to San Cristóbal with about three hundred heavily armed men, who then attacked the Indian force armed only with sticks and
       <a href="/wiki/Machete" title="Machete">
        machetes
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens91_40-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens91-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The indigenous force was quickly dispersed and routed with government troops pursuing pockets of guerrilla resistance in the mountains until 1870. The event effectively returned control of the indigenous workforce back to the highland elite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones32_29-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones32-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens9192_41-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens9192-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Porfiriato.2C_1876-1911">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Porfiriato,_1876-1911">
        Porfiriato, 1876-1911
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Porfiriato, 1876-1911">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">
        Porfirio Díaz
       </a>
       era at the end of the 19th century and beginning of the 20th was initially thwarted by regional bosses called
       <a href="/wiki/Cacique" title="Cacique">
        caciques
       </a>
       , bolstered by a wave of Spanish and mestizo farmers who migrated to the state and added to the elite group of wealthy landowning families.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There was some technological progress such as a highway from San Cristóbal to the Oaxaca border and the first telephone line in the 1880s, but Porfirian era economic reforms would not begin until 1891 with Governor
       <a href="/wiki/Emilio_Rabasa" title="Emilio Rabasa">
        Emilio Rabasa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones32_29-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones32-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This governor took on the local and regional caciques and centralized power into the state capital, which he moved from San Cristóbal de las Casas to Tuxtla in 1892.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones32_29-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones32-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens98_42-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens98-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He modernized public administration, transportation and promoted education.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Rabasa also introduced
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Telegraph" title="Telegraph">
        telegraph
       </a>
       , limited public schooling, sanitation and road construction, including a route from San Cristóbal to Tuxtla then Oaxaca, which signaled the beginning of favoritism of development in the central valley over the highlands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens99_43-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens99-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       He also changed state policies to favor foreign investment, favored large land mass consolidation for the production of cash crops such as henequen, rubber, guayule, cochineal and coffee.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens96_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens96-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Agricultural production boomed, especially coffee, which induced the construction of port facilities in
       <a href="/wiki/Tonal%C3%A1,_Chiapas" title="Tonalá, Chiapas">
        Tonalá
       </a>
       . The economic expansion and investment in roads also increased access to tropical commodities such as hardwoods, rubber and
       <a href="/wiki/Chicle" title="Chicle">
        chicle
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens99_43-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens99-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       These still required cheap and steady labor to be provided by the indigenous population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-higgens99_43-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-higgens99-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the end of the 19th century, the four main indigenous groups, Tzeltals, Tzotzils, Tojolabals and Ch’ols were living in "reducciones" or reservations, isolated from one another.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo105_45-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo105-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Conditions on the farms of the Porfirian era was serfdom, as bad if not worse than for other indigenous and mestizo populations leading to the
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">
        Mexican Revolution
       </a>
       . While this coming event would affect the state, Chiapas did not follow the uprisings in other areas that would end the Porfirian era.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones3233_46-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones3233-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Mexican" title="Japanese Mexican">
        Japanese immigration
       </a>
       to Mexico began in 1897 when the first thirty five migrants arrived in Chiapas to work on coffee farms, so that Mexico was the first Latin American country to receive organized Japanese immigration.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MOFA_47-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MOFA-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although this colony ultimately failed, there remains a small Japanese community in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acacoyagua" title="Acacoyagua">
        Acacoyagua
       </a>
       , Chiapas.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century_to_1960">
        Early 20th century to 1960
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early 20th century to 1960">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg/250px-Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg/375px-Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg/500px-Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palacio_de_Gobierno_de_Chiapas_en_Tuxtla.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The Palace of Government of Chiapas (Governor's Office) at Tuxtla Gutiérrez
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG/250px-AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG/375px-AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG/500px-AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AtriumCongresoTuxla.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Palacio Legislativo (Legislative Palace) at Tuxtla Gutiérrez
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3117" data-file-width="2227" height="308" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg/220px-SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg/330px-SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg/440px-SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SugarCaneMillTapachula.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Sugar cane mill from Tapachula on display at the Regional Museum in Chiapas
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In the early 20th century and into the
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Revolution" title="Mexican Revolution">
        Mexican Revolution
       </a>
       , the production of coffee was particularly important but labor-intensive. This would lead to a practice called
       <i>
        enganche
       </i>
       (hook), where recruiter would lure workers with advanced pay and other incentives such as alcohol and then trap them with debts for travel and other items to be worked off. This practice would lead to a kind of indentured servitude and uprisings in areas of the state, although they never led to large rebel armies as in other parts of Mexico.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martineztorres_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-martineztorres-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A small war broke out between
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
        Tuxtla Gutiérrez
       </a>
       and San Cristobal in 1911. San Cristóbal, allied with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Juan_Chamula" title="San Juan Chamula">
        San Juan Chamula
       </a>
       , tried to regain the state's capital but the effort failed. San Cristóbal de las Casas, who had a very limited budget, to the extent that it had to ally with San Juan Chamula, and Tuxtla Gutierrez, which was enough only a small ragtag army to beat overwhelmingly the army helped by chamulas from San Cristóbal. There were three years of peace after that until troops allied with "First Chief" of the revolutionary Constitutionalist forces,
       <a href="/wiki/Venustiano_Carranza" title="Venustiano Carranza">
        Venustiano Carranza
       </a>
       , entered in 1914 taking over the government, with the aim of imposing the
       <i>
        Ley de Obreros
       </i>
       (Workers' Law) to address injustices against the state's mostly indigenous workers. Conservatives responded violently months later when they were certain the Carranza forces would take their lands. This was mostly by way of guerrilla actions headed by farm owners who called themselves the
       <i>
        Mapaches
       </i>
       . This action continued for six years, until President Carranza was assassinated in 1920 and revolutionary general
       <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Obreg%C3%B3n" title="Álvaro Obregón">
        Álvaro Obregón
       </a>
       became president of Mexico. This allowed the Mapaches to gain political power in the state and effectively stop many of the social reforms occurring in other parts of Mexico.
      </p>
      <p>
       The Mapaches continued to fight against socialists and communists in Mexico from 1920 to 1936, to maintain their control over the state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones35_19-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones35-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In general, elite landowners also allied with the nationally dominant party founded by
       <a href="/wiki/Plutarco_El%C3%ADas_Calles" title="Plutarco Elías Calles">
        Plutarco Elías Calles
       </a>
       following the assassination of president-elect Obregón in 1928; that party was renamed the
       <a href="/wiki/Institutional_Revolutionary_Party" title="Institutional Revolutionary Party">
        Institutional Revolutionary Party
       </a>
       in 1946. Through that alliance, they could block land reform in this way as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Mapaches were first defeated in 1925 when an alliance of socialists and former Carranza loyalists had Carlos A. Vidal selected as governor, although he was assassinated two years later. The last of the Mapache resistance was over come in the early 1930s by Governor Victorico Grajales, who pursued President
       <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A1zaro_C%C3%A1rdenas" title="Lázaro Cárdenas">
        Lázaro Cárdenas
       </a>
       ' social and economic policies including persecution of the Catholic Church. These policies would have some success in redistributing lands and organizing indigenous workers but the state would remain relatively isolated for the rest of the 20th
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones35_19-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones35-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The territory was reorganized into municipalities in 1916. The current state constitution was written in 1921.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There was political stability from the 1940s to the early 1970s; however, regionalism regained with people thinking of themselves as from their local city or municipality over the state. This regionalism impeded the economy as local authorities restrained outside goods. For this reason, construction of highways and communications were pushed to help with economic development. Most of the work was done around Tuxtla Gutiérrez and Tapachula. This included the Sureste railroad connecting northern municipalities such as Pichucalco, Salto de Agua, Palenque,
       <a href="/wiki/Catazaj%C3%A1" title="Catazajá">
        Catazajá
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/La_Libertad,_Chiapas" title="La Libertad, Chiapas">
        La Libertad
       </a>
       . The Cristobal Colon highway linked Tuxtla to the Guatemalan border. Other highways included El Escopetazo to Pichucalco, a highway between San Cristóbal and Palenque with branches to Cuxtepeques and La
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       Frailesca. This helped to integrate the state's economy, but it also permitted the political rise of communal land owners called ejidatarios.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="825" data-file-width="1280" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG/220px-Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG/330px-Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG/440px-Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lacanja_burn_crop.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Area of the Lacandon Jungle burned to plant crops
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mid-20th_century_to_1990">
        Mid-20th century to 1990
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Mid-20th century to 1990">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In the mid-20th century, the state experienced a significant rise in population, which outstripped local resources, especially land in the highland areas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sspeed_48-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-sspeed-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the 1930s, many indigenous and mestizos have migrated from the highland areas into the
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_Jungle" title="Lacandon Jungle">
        Lacandon Jungle
       </a>
       with the populations of Altamirano, Las Margaritas, Ocosingo and Palenque rising from less than 11,000 in 1920 to over 376,000 in 2000. These migrants came to the jungle area to clear forest and grow crops and raise livestock, especially cattle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo106_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo106-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Economic development in general raised the output of the state, especially in agriculture, but it had the effect of deforesting many areas, especially the Lacandon. Added to this was there was still serf like conditions for many workers and insufficient educational infrastructure. Population continued to increase faster than the economy could absorb
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There were some attempts to resettle peasant farmers onto non cultivated lands, but they were met with resistance. President
       <a href="/wiki/Gustavo_D%C3%ADaz_Ordaz" title="Gustavo Díaz Ordaz">
        Gustavo Díaz Ordaz
       </a>
       awarded a land grant to the town of Venustiano Carranza in 1967, but that land was already being used by cattle-ranchers who refused to leave. The peasants tried to take over the land anyway, but when violence broke out, they were forcibly removed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamnett264_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamnett264-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In Chiapas poor farmland and severe poverty afflict the Mayan Indians which led to unsuccessful non violent protests and eventually armed struggle started by the Zapatista National Liberation Army in January 1994.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       These events began to lead to political crises in the 1970s, with more frequent land invasions and takeovers of municipal halls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-enchis_18-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-enchis-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamnett264_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamnett264-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was the beginning of a process that would lead to the emergence of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EZLN" title="EZLN">
        Zapatista movement
       </a>
       in the 1990s. Another important factor to this movement would be the role of the Catholic Church from the 1960s to the 1980s. In 1960,
       <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Ruiz" title="Samuel Ruiz">
        Samuel Ruiz
       </a>
       became the bishop of the Diocese of Chiapas, centered in San Cristóbal. He supported and worked with
       <a href="/wiki/Marist_Brothers" title="Marist Brothers">
        Marist priests and nuns
       </a>
       following an ideology called
       <a href="/wiki/Liberation_theology" title="Liberation theology">
        liberation theology
       </a>
       . In 1974, he organized a statewide "Indian Congress" with representatives from the Tzeltal, Tzotzil, Tojolabal and Ch'ol peoples from 327 communities as well as Marists and the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Maoist_People%27s_Union&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Maoist People's Union (page does not exist)">
        Maoist People's Union
       </a>
       . This congress was the first of its kind with the goal of uniting the indigenous peoples politically. These efforts were also supported by leftist organizations from outside Mexico, especially to form unions of
       <a href="/wiki/Ejido" title="Ejido">
        ejido
       </a>
       organizations. These unions would later form the base of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/EZLN" title="EZLN">
        EZLN
       </a>
       organization.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sspeed_48-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-sspeed-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One reason for the Church's efforts to reach out to the indigenous population was that starting in the 1970s, a shift began from traditional Catholic affiliation to Protestant, Evangelical and other Christian sects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo108_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo108-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The 1980s saw a large wave of refugees coming into the state from
       <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">
        Central America
       </a>
       as a number of these countries, especially
       <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">
        Guatemala
       </a>
       , were in the midst of violent political turmoil. The Chiapas/Guatemala border had been relatively porous with people traveling back and forth easily in the 19th and 20th
       <span class="nowrap">
       </span>
       centuries, much like the Mexico/U.S. border around the same time. This is in spite of tensions caused by Mexico's annexation of the
