       The 20th century saw large population growth in Chiapas. From fewer than one million inhabitants in 1940, the state had about two million in 1980, and over 4 million in 2005.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ben1_80-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ben1-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Overcrowded land in the highlands was relieved when the rainforest to the east was subject to land reform. Cattle ranchers, loggers, and subsistence farmers migrated to the rain forest area. The population of the Lacandon was only one thousand people in 1950, but by the mid-1990s this had increased to 200 thousand.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2010, 78% lives in urban communities with 22% in rural communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegidis_99-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-inegidis-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While birthrates are still high in the state, they have come down in recent decades from 7.4 per woman in 1950. However, these rates still mean significant population growth in raw numbers. About half of the state's population is under age 20, with an average age of 19.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2005, there were 924,967 households, 81% headed by men and the rest by women. Most households were nuclear families (70.7%) with 22.1% consisting of extended families.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegiviv_101-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-inegiviv-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       More migrate out of Chiapas than migrate in, with emigrants leaving for
       <a href="/wiki/Tabasco" title="Tabasco">
        Tabasco
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
        Oaxaca
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">
        Veracruz
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/State_of_Mexico" title="State of Mexico">
        State of Mexico
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">
        Federal District
       </a>
       primarily.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While Catholics remain the majority, their numbers have dropped as many have converted to Protestant denominations in recent decades.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/National_Presbyterian_Church_in_Mexico" title="National Presbyterian Church in Mexico">
        National Presbyterian Church in Mexico
       </a>
       has a large following in Chiapas; some estimate that 40% of the population are followers of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterian" title="Presbyterian">
        Presbyterian
       </a>
       church.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are a number of people in the state with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afro-Mexican" title="Afro-Mexican">
        African features
       </a>
       . These are the descendents of slaves brought to the state in the 16th century. There are also those with predominantly European features who are the descendents of the original Spanish colonizers as well as later immigrants to Mexico. The latter mostly came at the end of the 19th and early 20th century under the
       <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">
        Porfirio Díaz
       </a>
       regime to start plantations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Indigenous_population">
        Indigenous population
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Indigenous population">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers_and_influence">
        Numbers and influence
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Numbers and influence">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       Over the history of Chiapas, there have been three main indigenous groups: the
       <a href="/wiki/Mixe_people" title="Mixe people">
        Mixes
       </a>
       -
       <a href="/wiki/Zoque_people" title="Zoque people">
        Zoques
       </a>
       , the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayan_peoples" title="Mayan peoples">
        Mayas
       </a>
       and the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapa_people&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chiapa people (page does not exist)">
        Chiapa
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, there are an estimated fifty-six linguistic groups. As of the 2005 Census, there were 957,255 people who spoke an indigenous language out of a total population of about 3.5 million. Of this one million, one third do not speak Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamnett18_104-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamnett18-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Out of Chiapas' 111 municipios, ninety-nine have significant indigenous populations.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Twenty two municipalities have indigenous populations over 90 percent, and 36 municipalities have native populations exceeding 50 percent. However, despite population growth in indigenous villages, the percentage of indigenous to non indigenous continues to fall with less than 35% indigenous. Indian populations are concentrated in a few areas, with the largest concentration of indigenous-language-speaking individuals is living in five of Chiapas's nine economic regions: Los Altos, Selva, Norte, Fronteriza, and Sierra. The remaining four regions, Centro, Frailesca, Soconusco, and Costa, have populations that are considered to be dominantly
       <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">
        mestizo
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The state has about 13.5% of all of Mexico's indigenous population,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and it has been ranked among the ten "most indianized" states, with only
       <a href="/wiki/Campeche" title="Campeche">
        Campeche
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
        Oaxaca
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Quintana_Roo" title="Quintana Roo">
        Quintana Roo
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n" title="Yucatán">
        Yucatán
       </a>
       having been ranked above it between 1930 and the present.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo109_105-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo109-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These indigenous peoples have been historically resistant to assimilation into the broader Mexican society, with it best seen in the retention rates of indigenous languages and the historic demands for autonomy over geographic areas as well as cultural domains. Much of the latter has been prominent since the Zapatista uprising in 1994.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo119_106-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo119-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most of Chiapas' indigenous groups are descended from the Mayans, speaking languages that are closely related to one another, belonging to the
       <a href="/wiki/Mayan_languages" title="Mayan languages">
        Western Maya language group
       </a>
       . The state was part of a large region dominated by the Mayans during the
       <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_chronology" title="Mesoamerican chronology">
        Classic period
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most numerous of these Mayan groups include the
       <a href="/wiki/Tzeltal_people" title="Tzeltal people">
        Tzeltal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tzotzil_people" title="Tzotzil people">
        Tzotzil
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ch%27ol_people" title="Ch'ol people">
        Ch'ol
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Zoque_people" title="Zoque people">
        Zoque
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tojolabal_people" title="Tojolabal people">
        Tojolabal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_people" title="Lacandon people">
        Lacandon
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mam_people" title="Mam people">
        Mam
       </a>
       , which have traits in common such as syncretic religious practices, and social structure based on kinship.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-costumbresdes_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-costumbresdes-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most common Western Maya languages are
       <a href="/wiki/Tzeltal_language" title="Tzeltal language">
        Tzeltal
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tzotzil_language" title="Tzotzil language">
        Tzotzil
       </a>
       along with
       <a href="/wiki/Chontal_Maya_language" title="Chontal Maya language">
        Chontal
       </a>
       , Ch’ol, Tojolabal, Chuj, Kanjobal, Acatec, Jacaltec and Motozintlec.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Twelve of Mexico's officially recognized native peoples live in the state have conserved their language, customs, history dress and traditions to a significant degree. The primary groups include the Tzeltal, Tzotzil,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ch%27ol_language" title="Ch'ol language">
        Ch'ol
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tojolab%27al_language" title="Tojolab'al language">
        Tojolabal
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoque_language" title="Zoque language">
        Zoque
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chuj_language" title="Chuj language">
        Chuj
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Q%27anjob%27al_people" title="Q'anjob'al people">
        Kanjobal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mam_language" title="Mam language">
        Mam
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacalteco" title="Jacalteco">
        Jacalteco
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Moch%C3%B3_Cakchiquel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mochó Cakchiquel (page does not exist)">
        Mochó Cakchiquel
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_language" title="Lacandon language">
        Lacandon
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ubicagob_77-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ubicagob-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones18_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones18-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most indigenous communities are found in the municipalities of the Centro, Altos, Norte and Selva regions, with many having indigenous populations of over fifty percent. These include
       <a href="/wiki/Bochil" title="Bochil">
        Bochil
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sital%C3%A1" title="Sitalá">
        Sitalá
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pantepec,_Chiapas" title="Pantepec, Chiapas">
        Pantepec
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Simojovel" title="Simojovel">
        Simojovel
       </a>
       to those with over ninety percent indigenous such as
       <a href="/wiki/San_Juan_Cancuc" title="San Juan Cancuc">
        San Juan Cancuc
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Huixt%C3%A1n" title="Huixtán">
        Huixtán
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenejapa" title="Tenejapa">
        Tenejapa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tila,_Chiapas" title="Tila, Chiapas">
        Tila
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Oxchuc" title="Oxchuc">
        Oxchuc
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tapalapa" title="Tapalapa">
        Tapalapa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Zinacant%C3%A1n" title="Zinacantán">
        Zinacantán
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mitontic" title="Mitontic">
        Mitontic
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ocotepec,_Chiapas" title="Ocotepec, Chiapas">
        Ocotepec
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chamula" title="Chamula">
        Chamula
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Chalchihuit%C3%A1n" title="Chalchihuitán">
        Chalchihuitán
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most numerous indigenous communities are the Tzeltal and Tzotzil peoples, who number about 400,000 each, together accounting for about half of the state's indigenous population. The next most numerous are the Ch’ol with about 200,000 people and the Tojolabal and Zoques, who number about 50,000 each.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The top 3 municipalities in Chiapas with indigenous language speakers 3 years of age and older are: Ocosingo (133,811), Chilon (96,567), and San Juan Chamula (69,475). These three municipalities accounted for 24.8% (299,853) of all indigenous language speakers 3 years or older in the state of Chiapas, out of a total of 1,209,057 indigenous language speakers 3 years or older.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although most indigenous language speakers are bilingual, especially in the younger generations, many of these languages have shown resilience. Four of Chiapas' indigenous languages Tzeltal, Tzotzil, Tojolabal and Chol are high-vitality languages, meaning that a high percentage of these ethnicities speak the language and that there is a high rate of monolingualism in it. It is used in over 80% of homes. Zoque is considered to be of medium-vitality with a rate of bilingualism of over 70% and home use somewhere between 65% and 80%. Maya is considered to be of low-vitality with almost all of its speakers bilingual with Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo19_111-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo19-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most spoken indigenous languages as of 2010 are Tzeltal with 461,236 speakers, Tzotzil with 417,462, Ch’ol with 191,947 and Zoque with 53,839. In total, there are 1,141,499 who speak an indigenous language or 27% of the total population. Of these 14% do not speak Spanish.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegidiv_112-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-inegidiv-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Studies done between 1930 and 2000 have indicated that Spanish is not dramatically displacing these languages. In raw number, speakers of these languages are increasing, especially among groups with a long history of resistance to Spanish/Mexican domination.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo109_105-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo109-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Language maintenance has been strongest in areas related to where the Zapatista uprising took plaza such as the municipalities of Altamirano, Chamula,
       <a href="/wiki/Chanal" title="Chanal">
        Chanal
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Larr%C3%A1inzar" title="Larráinzar">
        Larráinzar
       </a>
       , Las Margaritas, Ocosingo, Palenque,
       <a href="/wiki/Sabanilla" title="Sabanilla">
        Sabanilla
       </a>
       , San Cristóbal de Las Casas and Simojovel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo117_113-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo117-113">
         [113]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The state's rich indigenous tradition along with its associated political uprisings, especially that of 1994, has great interest from other parts of Mexico and abroad.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has been especially appealing to a variety of academics including many anthropologists, archeologists, historians, psychologists and sociologists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The concept of "
       <a href="/wiki/Mestizo" title="Mestizo">
        mestizo
       </a>
       " or mixed indigenous European heritage became important to Mexico's identity by the time of Independence, but Chiapas has kept its indigenous identity to the present day.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since the 1970s, this has been supported by the Mexican government as it has shifted from cultural policies that favor a "multicultural" identity for the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosalva_114-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rosalva-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One major exception to the separatist, indigenous identity has been the case of the Chiapa people, from whom the state's name comes, who have mostly been assimilated and intermarried into the mestizo population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Most Indigenous communities have economies based primarily on traditional agriculture such as the cultivation and processing of corn, beans and coffee as a cash crop and in the last decade, many have begun producing sugarcane and
       <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">
        jatropha
       </a>
       for refinement into biodiesel and ethanol for automobile fuel.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         [115]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
        <a href="#cite_note-116">
         [116]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The raising of livestock, particularly chicken and turkey and to a lesser extent beef and farmed fish is also a major economic activity. Many indigenous, in particular the Maya are employed in the production of traditional clothing, fabrics, textiles, wood items, artworks and traditional goods such as jade and amber works.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bosques_117-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bosques-117">
         [117]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tourism has provided a number of a these communities with markets for their handcrafts and works, some of which are very profitable.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-perfilsoc_100-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-perfilsoc-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       San Cristóbal de las Casas and San Juan Chamula maintain a strong indigenous identity. On market day, many indigenous people from rural areas come into San Cristóbal to buy and sell mostly items for everyday use such as fruit, vegetables, animals, cloth, consumer goods and tools.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-costumbresdes_107-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-costumbresdes-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       San Juan Chamula is considered to be a center of indigenous culture, especially its elaborate festivals of
       <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">
        Carnival
       </a>
       and Day of Saint John. It was common for politicians, especially during
       <a href="/wiki/Institutional_Revolutionary_Party" title="Institutional Revolutionary Party">
        Institutional Revolutionary Party
       </a>
       's dominance to visit here during election campaigns and dress in indigenous clothing and carry a carved walking stick, a traditional sign of power.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Relations between the indigenous ethnic groups is complicated. While there have been inter ethnic political activism such as that promoted by the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_Las_Casas" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of San Cristóbal de Las Casas">
        Diocese of Chiapas
       </a>
       in the 1970s and the
       <a href="/wiki/Zapatista_Army_of_National_Liberation" title="Zapatista Army of National Liberation">
        Zapatista movement
       </a>
       in the 1990s, there has been inter-indigenous conflict as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stevenson_72-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-stevenson-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Much of this has been based on religion, pitting those of the traditional Catholic/indigenous beliefs who support the traditional power structure against Protestants, Evangelicals and Word of God Catholics (directly allied with the Diocese) who tend to oppose it. This is particularly significant problem among the Tzeltals and Tzotzils. Starting in the 1970s, traditional leaders in San Juan Chamula began expelling dissidents from their homes and land, amounting to about 20,000 indigenous forced to leave over a thirty-year period. It continues to be a serious social problem although authorities downplay it.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo105_45-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo105-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Recently there has been political, social and ethnic conflict between the Tzotzil who are more urbanized and have a significant number of Protestant practitioners and the Tzeltal who are predominantly Catholic and live in smaller farming communities. Many Protestant Tzotzil have accused the Tzeltal of ethnic discrimination and intimidation due to their religious beliefs and the Tzeltal have in return accused the Tzotzil of singling them out for discrimination.
      </p>
      <p>
       Clothing, especially women’s clothing, varies by indigenous group. For example, women in Ocosingo tend to wear a blouse with a round collar embroidered with flowers and a black skirt decorated with ribbons and tied with a cloth belt. The Lacandon people tend to wear a simple white tunic. They also make a ceremonial tunic from
       <a href="/wiki/Amate" title="Amate">
        bark
       </a>
       , decorated with astronomy symbols. In Tenejapa, women wear a
       <a href="/wiki/Huipil" title="Huipil">
        huipil
       </a>
       embroidered with Mayan fretwork along with a black wool
       <a href="/wiki/Rebozo" title="Rebozo">
        rebozo
       </a>
       . Men wear short pants, embroidered at the bottom.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artepopdes_118-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-artepopdes-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tzeltals">
        Tzeltals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Tzeltals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Tzeltals call themselves Winik atel, which means "working men." This is the largest ethnicity in the state, mostly living southeast of San Cristóbal with the largest number in
       <a href="/wiki/Amatenango_del_Valle" title="Amatenango del Valle">
        Amatenango
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, there are about 500,000 Tzeltal Indians in Chiapas. Tzeltal Mayan, part of the Mayan language family, today is spoken by about 375,000 people making it the fourth-largest language group in Mexico. There are two main dialects; highland (or Oxchuc) and lowland (or Bachajonteco) .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This language, along with Tzotzil, is from the Tzeltalan subdivision of the Mayan language family. Lexico-statistical studies indicate that these two languages probably became differentiated from one another around 1200
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most children are bilingual in the language and Spanish although many of their grandparents are monolingual Tzeltal speakers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Each Tzeltal community constitutes a distinct social and cultural unit with its own well-defined lands, wearing apparel, kinship system, politico-religious organization, economic resources, crafts, and other cultural features.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Women are distinguished by a black skirt with a wool belt and an undyed cotton bloused embroidered with flowers. Their hair is tied with ribbons and covered with a cloth. Most men do not use traditional attire.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Agriculture is the basic economic activity of the Tzeltal people. Traditional
       <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">
        Mesoamerican
       </a>
       crops such as maize, beans, squash, and
       <a href="/wiki/Chili_pepper" title="Chili pepper">
        chili peppers
       </a>
       are the most important, but a variety of other crops, including wheat,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manioc" title="Manioc">
        manioc
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">
        sweet potatoes
       </a>
       , cotton,
       <a href="/wiki/Chayote" title="Chayote">
        chayote
       </a>
       , some fruits, other vegetables, and coffee.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jrodriguez_24-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-jrodriguez-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tzotzils">
        Tzotzils
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Tzotzils">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg/220px-San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg/330px-San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg/440px-San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:San_Cristobal_-_Indianische_Stra%C3%9Fenh%C3%A4ndlerinnen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Tzotzil women in San Cristobal de las Casas
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Tzotzil speakers number just slightly less than theTzeltals at 226,000, although those of the ethnicity are probably higher.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tzotzils are found in the highlands or Los Altos and spread out towards the northeast near the border with Tabasco. However, Tzotzil communities can be found in almost every municipality of the state. They are concentrated in Chamula, Zinacantán,
       <a href="/wiki/Chenalh%C3%B3" title="Chenalhó">
        Chenalhó
       </a>
       , and Simojovel. Their language is closely related to Tzeltal and distantly related to
       <a href="/wiki/Yucatec_Maya_language" title="Yucatec Maya language">
        Yucatec Mayan
       </a>
       and Lacandon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo108_52-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo108-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Men dress in short pants tied with a red cotton belt and a shirt that hangs down to their knees. They also wear leather huaraches and a hat decorated with ribbons. The women wear a red or blue skirt, a short huipil as a blouse, and use a chal or
       <a href="/wiki/Rebozo" title="Rebozo">
        rebozo
       </a>
       to carry babies and bundles. Tzotzil communities are governed by a katinab who is selected for life by the leaders of each neighborhood. The Tzotzils are also known for their continued use of the
       <a href="/wiki/Temazcal" title="Temazcal">
        temazcal
       </a>
       for hygiene and medicinal purposes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Ch.E2.80.99ols">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ch’ols">
        Ch’ols
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ch’ols">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2338" data-file-width="1497" height="344" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG/220px-IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG/330px-IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG/440px-IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IndigenousWomanPalenque2.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Ch'ol woman in Palenque
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Ch%27ol_people" title="Ch'ol people">
        Ch’ols of Chiapas
       </a>
       migrated to the northwest of the state starting about 2,000 years ago, when they were concentrated in Guatemala and
       <a href="/wiki/Honduras" title="Honduras">
        Honduras
       </a>
       . Those Ch’ols who remained in the south are distinguished by the name
       <a href="/wiki/Ch%27orti%27_people" title="Ch'orti' people">
        Chortís
       </a>
       . Chiapas Ch’ols are closely related to the Chontal in Tabasco as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Choles are found in Tila, Tumbalá, Sabanilla, Palenque, and
       <a href="/wiki/Salto_de_Agua" title="Salto de Agua">
        Salto de Agua
       </a>
       , with an estimated population of about 115,000 people.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Ch’ol language belongs to the Maya family and is related to Tzeltal, Tzotzil, Lacandon, Tojolabal, and Yucatec Mayan. There are three varieties of Chol (spoken in Tila, Tumbalá, and Sabanilla), all mutually intelligible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo108_52-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo108-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Over half of speakers are monolingual in the Chol language. Women wear a long navy blue or black skirt with a white blouse heavily embroidered with bright colors and a sash with a red ribbon. The men only occasionally use traditional dress for events such as the feast of the Virgin of Guadalupe. This dress usually includes pants, shirts and huipils made of undyed cotton, with leather
       <a href="/wiki/Huarache_(shoe)" title="Huarache (shoe)">
        huaraches
       </a>
       , a carrying sack and a hat.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The fundamental economic activity of the Ch’ols is agriculture. They primarily cultivate corn and beans, as well as sugar cane, rice, coffee, and some fruits.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They have Catholic beliefs strongly influenced by native ones. Harvests are celebrated on the Feast of Saint Rose on 30 August.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tojolabals">
        Tojolabals
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Tojolabals">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Totolabals are estimated at 35,000 in the highlands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones21_120-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones21-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to oral tradition, the Tojolabales came north from Guatemala.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The largest community is Ingeniero González de León in the La Cañada region, an hour outside the municipal seat of Las Margaritas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones21_120-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones21-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tojolabales are also found in Comitán, Trinitaria, Altamirano and La Independencia.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo109_105-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo109-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This area is filled with rolling hills with a temperate and moist climate. There are fast moving rivers and jungle vegetation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones21_120-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones21-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tojolabal is related to Kanjobal, but also to Tzeltal and Tzotzil.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo109_105-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo109-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, most of the youngest of this ethnicity speak Spanish. Women dress traditionally from childhood with brightly colored skirts decorated with lace or ribbons and a blouse decorated with small ribbons, and they cover their heads with kerchiefs. They embroider many of their own clothes but do not sell them. Married women arrange their hair in two braids and single women wear it loose decorated with ribbons. Men no longer wear traditional garb daily as it is considered too expensive to make.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones21_120-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones21-120">
         [120]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Zoques">
        Zoques
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Zoques">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Zoques are found in 3,000 square kilometers the center and west of the state scattered among hundreds of communities. These were one of the first native peoples of Chiapas, with archeological ruins tied to them dating back as far as 3500 BCE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Their language is not Mayan but rather related to
       <a href="/wiki/Mixe_languages" title="Mixe languages">
        Mixe
       </a>
       , which is found in
       <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
        Oaxaca
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Veracruz" title="Veracruz">
        Veracruz
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo109_105-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo109-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       By the time the Spanish arrived, they had been reduced in number and territory. Their ancient capital was Quechula, which was covered with water by the creation of the Malpaso Dam, along with the ruins of Guelegas, which was first buried by an eruption of the
       <a href="/wiki/El_Chich%C3%B3n" title="El Chichón">
        Chichonal volcano
       </a>
       . There are still Zoque ruins at Janepaguay, the Ocozocuautla and La Ciénega valleys.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Lacandons">
        Lacandons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Lacandons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Lacandons are one of the smallest native indigenous groups of the state with a population estimated between 600 and 1000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1921_121-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones1921-121">
         [121]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They are mostly located in the communities of Lacanjá, Chansayab and Mensabak in the
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_Jungle" title="Lacandon Jungle">
        Lacandon Jungle
       </a>
       . They live near the ruins of
       <a href="/wiki/Bonampak" title="Bonampak">
        Bonampak
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Yaxchilan" title="Yaxchilan">
        Yaxchilan
       </a>
       and local lore states that the gods resided here when they lived on Earth. They inhabit about a million hectares of rainforest but from the 16th century to the present, migrants have taken over the area, most of which are indigenous from other areas of Chiapas. This dramatically altered their lifestyle and worldview. Traditional Lacandon shelters are huts made with fonds and wood with an earthen floor, but this has mostly given way to modern structures.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones20_119-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones20-119">
         [119]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span id="Moch.C3.B3s">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mochós">
        Mochós
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Mochós">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Mochós or Motozintlecos are concentrated in the municipality of
       <a href="/wiki/Motozintla" title="Motozintla">
        Motozintla
       </a>
       on the Guatemalan border. According to anthropologists, these people are an "urban" ethnicity as they are mostly found in the neighborhoods of the municipal seat. Other communities can be found near the
       <a href="/wiki/Volc%C3%A1n_Tacan%C3%A1" title="Volcán Tacaná">
        Tacaná volcano
       </a>
       , and in the municipalities of
       <a href="/wiki/Tuzant%C3%A1n" title="Tuzantán">
        Tuzantán
       </a>
       and Belisario Dominguez. The name "Mochó" comes from a response many gave the Spanish whom they could not understand and means "I don't know." This community is in the process of disappearing as their numbers shrink.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones22_122-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones22-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mams">
        Mams
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Mams">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The Mams are a Mayan ethnicity that numbers about 20,000 found in thirty municipalities, especially
       <a href="/wiki/Tapachula" title="Tapachula">
        Tapachula
       </a>
       , Motozintla, El Porvenir,
       <a href="/wiki/Cacahoat%C3%A1n" title="Cacahoatán">
        Cacahoatán
       </a>
       and Amatenango in the southeastern Sierra Madre of Chiapas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosalva_114-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rosalva-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones22_122-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones22-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Mame language is one of the most ancient Mayan languages with 5,450 Mame speakers were tallied in Chiapas in the 2000 census.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These people first migrated to the border region between Chiapas and Guatemala at the end of the nineteenth century, establishing scattered settlements. In the 1960s, several hundred migrated to the Lacandon rain forest near the confluence of the Santo Domingo and Jataté Rivers. Those who live in Chiapas are referred to locally as the "Mexican Mam (or Mame)" to differientiate them from those in Guatemala.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosalva_114-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rosalva-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most live around the Tacaná volcano, which the Mams call "our mother" as it is considered to be the source of the fertility of the area's fields. The masculine deity is the Tajumulco volcano, which is in Guatemala.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rosalva_114-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-rosalva-114">
         [114]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones22_122-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones22-122">
         [122]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Guatemalan_migrant_groups">
        Guatemalan migrant groups
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Guatemalan migrant groups">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       In the last decades of the 20th century, Chiapas received a large number of indigenous refugees, especially from Guatemala, many of whom remain in the state. These have added ethnicities such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Q%27eqchi%27_people" title="Q'eqchi' people">
        Kekchi
       </a>
       , Chuj,
       <a href="/wiki/Ixil_people" title="Ixil people">
        Ixil
       </a>
       , Kanjobal,
       <a href="/wiki/K%27iche%27_people" title="K'iche' people">
        K'iche'
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kaqchikel_people" title="Kaqchikel people">
        Cakchikel
       </a>
       to the population.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones19_103-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones19-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Kanjobal mainly live along the border between Chiapas and Guatemala, with almost 5,800 speakers of the language tallied in the 2000 census. It is believed that a significant number of these Kanjobal-speakers may have been born in Guatemala and immigrated to Chiapas, maintaining strong cultural ties to the neighboring nation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">
        Economy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Economy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_indicators">
        Economic indicators
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Economic indicators">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Chiapas accounts for 1.73% of Mexico's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">
        GDP
       </a>
       . The primary sector, agriculture, produces 15.2% of the states GDP. The secondary sector, mostly energy production, but also commerce, services and tourism, accounts for 21.8%. The share of the GDP coming from services is rising while that of agriculture is falling.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The state is divided into nine economic regions. These regions were established in the 1980s in order to facilitate statewide economic planning. Many of these regions are based on state and federal highway systems. These include Centro, Altos, Fronteriza, Frailesca, Norte, Selva, Sierra, Soconusco and Istmo-Costa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-regionalizacion_124-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-regionalizacion-124">
         [124]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Despite being rich in resources, Chiapas, along with
       <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
        Oaxaca
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Guerrero" title="Guerrero">
        Guerrero
       </a>
       , lags behind the rest of the country in almost all socioeconomic indicators.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adultwomen_71-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-adultwomen-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2005
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , there were 889,420 residential units; 71% had running water, 77.3% sewerage, and 93.6% electricity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegiviv_101-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-inegiviv-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Construction of these units varies from modern construction of block and concrete to those constructed of wood and laminate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_125-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-infraes-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Because of its high rate of economic marginalization, more people migrate from Chiapas than migrate to it. Most of its socioeconomic indicators are the lowest in the country including income, education, health and housing. It has a significantly higher percentage of illiteracy than the rest of the country, although that situation has improved since the 1970s when over 45% were illiterate and 1980s, about 32%. The tropical climate presents health challenges, with most illnesses related to the gastro-intestinal tract and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">
        parasites
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pimienta_76-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pimienta-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2005, the state has 1,138 medical facilities: 1098 outpatient and 40 inpatient. Most are run by IMSS and ISSSTE and other government agencies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_125-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-infraes-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The implementation of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NAFTA" title="NAFTA">
        NAFTA
       </a>
       had negative effects on the economy, particularly by lowering prices for agricultural products. It made the southern states of Mexico poorer in comparison to those in the north, with over 90% of the poorest municipalities in the south of the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adultwomen_71-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-adultwomen-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of 2006, 31.8% work in communal services, social services and personal services. 18.4% work in financial services, insurance and real estate, 10.7% work in commerce, restaurants and hotels, 9.8% work in construction, 8.9% in utilities, 7.8% in transportation, 3.4% in industry (excluding
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_handcrafts_and_folk_art" title="Mexican handcrafts and folk art">
        handcrafts
       </a>
       ), and 8.4% in agriculture.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegiactecon_126-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-inegiactecon-126">
         [126]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Although until the 1960s, many indigenous communities were considered by scholars to be autonomous and economically isolated, this was never the case. Economic conditions began forcing many to migrate to work, especially in agriculture for non-indigenous. However, unlike many other migrant workers, most indigenous in Chiapas have remained strongly tied to their home communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A study as early as the 1970s showed that 77 percent of heads of household migrated outside of the
       <a href="/wiki/Chamula" title="Chamula">
        Chamula
       </a>
       municipality as local land did not produce sufficiently to support families. In the 1970s, cuts in the price of corn forced many large landowners to convert their fields into pasture for cattle, displacing many hired laborers, cattle required less work. These agricultural laborers began to work for the government on infrastructure projects financed by oil revenue.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is estimated that in the 1980s to 1990s as many as 100,000 indigenous people moved from the mountain areas into cities in Chiapas, with some moving out of the state to
       <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">
        Mexico City
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Canc%C3%BAn" title="Cancún">
        Cancún
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Villahermosa" title="Villahermosa">
        Villahermosa
       </a>
       in search of employment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Agriculture.2C_livestock.2C_forestry_and_fishing">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture,_livestock,_forestry_and_fishing">
        Agriculture, livestock, forestry and fishing
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Agriculture, livestock, forestry and fishing">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG/220px-RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG/330px-RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG/440px-RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RanchPalenqueChiapas.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Ranch near Palenque
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Agriculture, livestock, forestry and fishing employ over 53% of the state’s population; however, its productivity is considered to be low. Agriculture includes both seasonal and perennial plants. Major crops include corn, beans,
       <a href="/wiki/Sorghum" title="Sorghum">
        sorghum
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Soybean" title="Soybean">
        soybeans
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">
        peanuts
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sesame_seed" title="Sesame seed">
        sesame seeds
       </a>
       , coffee,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cacao_bean" title="Cacao bean">
        cacao
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sugar_cane" title="Sugar cane">
        sugar cane
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mango" title="Mango">
        mangos
       </a>
       , bananas, and
       <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">
        palm oil
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These crops take up 95% of the cultivated land in the state and 90% of the agricultural production. Only four percent of fields are irrigated with the rest dependent on rainfall either seasonally or year round.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Chiapas ranks second among the Mexican states in the production of cacao, the product used to make chocolate, and is responsible for about 60 percent of Mexico's total coffee output.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cafeorganico_127-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cafeorganico-127">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The production of bananas, cacao and corn make Chiapas Mexico's second largest agricultural producer overall.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Coffee is the state's most important cash crop with a history from the 19th century. The crop was introduced in 1846 by Jeronimo Manchinelli who brought 1,500 seedlings from Guatemala on his farm La Chacara. This was followed by a number of other farms as well. Coffee production intensified during the regime of
       <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">
        Porfirio Díaz
       </a>
       and the Europeans who came to own many of the large farms in the area. By 1892, there were 22 coffee farms in the region, among them Nueva Alemania, Hamburgo, Chiripa, Irlanda, Argovia, San Francisco, and Linda Vista in the
       <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">
        Soconusco
       </a>
       region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abianco_83-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-abianco-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Since then coffee production has grown and diversified to include large plantations, the use and free and forced labor and a significant sector of small producers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martineztorres_34-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-martineztorres-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While most coffee is grown in the Soconusco, other areas grow it, including the municipalities of
       <a href="/wiki/Oxchuc" title="Oxchuc">
        Oxchuc
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Panthel%C3%B3&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Pantheló (page does not exist)">
        Pantheló
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/El_Bosque,_Chiapas" title="El Bosque, Chiapas">
        El Bosque
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenejapa" title="Tenejapa">
        Tenejapa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Chenalh%C3%B3" title="Chenalhó">
        Chenalhó
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Larr%C3%A1inzar" title="Larráinzar">
        Larráinzar
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Chalchihuit%C3%A1n" title="Chalchihuitán">
        Chalchihuitán
       </a>
       , with around six thousand producers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-martineztorres_34-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-martineztorres-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It also includes organic coffee producers with 18 million tons grown annually 60,000 producers. One third of these producers are indigenous women and other peasant farmers who grow the coffee under the shade of native trees without the use of agro chemicals. Some of this coffee is even grown in environmentally protected areas such as the El Triunfo reserve, where ejidos with 14,000 people grow the coffee and sell it to cooperativers who sell it to companies such as
       <a href="/wiki/Starbucks" title="Starbucks">
        Starbucks
       </a>
       , but the main market is Europe. Some growers have created cooperatives of their own to cut out the middleman.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cafeorganico_127-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-cafeorganico-127">
         [127]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Ranching occupies about three million hectares of natural and induced pasture, with about 52% of all pasture induced. Most livestock is done by families using traditional methods. Most important are meat and dairy cattle, followed by pigs and domestic fowl. These three account for 93% of the value of production.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Annual milk production in Chiapas totals about 180 million liters per year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The state's cattle production, along with timber from the Lacandon Jungle and energy output gives it a certain amount of economic clouts compared to other states in the region.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Forestry is mostly based on conifers and common tropical species producing 186,858 m3 per year at a value of 54,511,000 pesos. Exploited non-wood species include the Camedor palm tree for its fronds. The fishing industry is underdeveloped but includes the capture of wild species as well as fish farming. Fish production is generated both from the ocean as well as the many freshwater rivers and lakes. In 2002, 28,582 tons of fish valued at 441.2 million pesos was produced. Species include
       <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">
        tuna
       </a>
       , shark, shrimp,
       <a href="/wiki/Mojarra" title="Mojarra">
        mojarra
       </a>
       and crab.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_and_energy">
        Industry and energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Industry and energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The state's abundant rivers and streams have been dammed to provide about fifty five percent of the country's hydroelectric energy. Much of this is sent to other states accounting for over six percent of all of Mexico's energy output.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediofisico_78-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-mediofisico-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo112113_128-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo112113-128">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Main power stations are located at Malpaso, La Angostura, Chicoasén and Peñitas, which produce about eight percent of Mexico's hydroelectric energy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediofisico_78-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-mediofisico-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Chicoas%C3%A9n_Dam" title="Chicoasén Dam">
        Manuel Moreno Torres plant
       </a>
       on the Grijalva River the most productive in Mexico. All of the hydroelectric plants are owned and operated by the Federal Electricity Commission (
       <a href="/wiki/Comisi%C3%B3n_Federal_de_Electricidad" title="Comisión Federal de Electricidad">
        Comisión Federal de Electricidad
       </a>
       , CFE).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CFE_79-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-CFE-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Chiapas is rich in petroleum reserves. Oil production began during the 1980s and Chiapas has become the fourth largest producer of crude oil and natural gas among the Mexican states.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many reserves are yet untapped, but between 1984 and 1992, PEMEX drilled nineteen oil wells in the Lacandona Jungle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo112113_128-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo112113-128">
         [128]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Currently, petroleum reserves are found in the municipalities of Juárez,
       <a href="/wiki/Ostuac%C3%A1n" title="Ostuacán">
        Ostuacán
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Pichucalco" title="Pichucalco">
        Pichucalco
       </a>
       and Reforma in the north of the state with 116 wells accounting for about 6.5% of the country's oil production. It also provides about a quarter of the country’s natural gas. This production equals 6,313.6 cubic metres (222,960 cu ft) of natural gas and 17,565,000 barrels of oil per year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mediofisico_78-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-mediofisico-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Industry is limited to small and micro enterprises and include auto parts, bottling, fruit packing, coffee and chocolate processing, production of
       <a href="/wiki/Lime_(material)" title="Lime (material)">
        lime
       </a>
       , bricks and other construction materials, sugar mills, furniture making, textiles, printing and the production of handcrafts. The two largest enterprises is the
       <a href="/wiki/Comisi%C3%B3n_Federal_de_Electricidad" title="Comisión Federal de Electricidad">
        Comisión Federal de Electricidad
       </a>
       and a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petr%C3%B3leos_Mexicanos" title="Petróleos Mexicanos">
        Petróleos Mexicanos
       </a>
       refinery.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Chiapas opened its first assembly plant in 2002, a fact that highlights the historical lack of industry in this area.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Handcrafts">
        Handcrafts
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Handcrafts">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG/220px-TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG/330px-TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG/440px-TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TextileStallSantoDomingoSanCris02.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Textiles for sale at Santo Domingo in San Cristóbal
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Chiapas is one of the states that produces a wide variety of
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_handcrafts_and_folk_art" title="Mexican handcrafts and folk art">
        handcrafts and folk art in Mexico
       </a>
       . One reason for this is its many indigenous ethnicities who produce traditional items out of identity as well as commercial reasons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones42_129-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones42-129">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One commercial reason is the market for crafts provided by the tourism industry. Another is that most indigenous communities can no longer provide for their own needs through agriculture. The need to generate outside income has led to many indigenous women producing crafts communally, which has not only had economic benefits but also involved them in the political process as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Unlike many other states, Chiapas has a wide variety of wood resources such as
       <a href="/wiki/Cedar_wood" title="Cedar wood">
        cedar
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Mahogany" title="Mahogany">
        mahogany
       </a>
       as well as plant species such as reeds,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ixtle" title="Ixtle">
        ixtle
       </a>
       and palm. It also has minerals such as
       <a href="/wiki/Obsidian" title="Obsidian">
        obsidian
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">
        amber
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">
        jade
       </a>
       and several types of clay and animals for the production of leather, dyes from various insects used to create the colors associated with the region. Items include various types of handcrafted clothing, dishes, jars, furniture, roof tiles, toys, musical instruments, tools and more.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones42_129-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones42-129">
         [129]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Chiapas’ most important handcraft is
       <a href="/wiki/Textiles_of_Mexico" title="Textiles of Mexico">
        textiles
       </a>
       , most of which is cloth woven on a backstrap loom.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artepopdes_118-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-artepopdes-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Indigenous girls often learn how to sew and embroider before they learn how to speak Spanish. They are also taught how to make natural dyes from insects, and weaving techniques. Many of the items produced are still for day-to-day use, often dyed in bright colors with intricate embroidery. They include skirts, belts,
       <a href="/wiki/Rebozo" title="Rebozo">
        rebozos
       </a>
       , blouses,
       <a href="/wiki/Huipil" title="Huipil">
        huipils
       </a>
       and shoulder wraps called chals. Designs are in red, yellow, turquoise blue, purple, pink, green and various pastels and decorated with designs such as flowers, butterflies, and birds, all based on local flora and fauna. Commercially, indigenous textiles are most often found in San Cristóbal de las Casas, San Juan Chamula and Zinacantán. The best textiles are considered to be from Magdalenas, Larráinzar, Venustiano Carranza and Sibaca.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones43_130-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones43-130">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG/220px-PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG/330px-PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG/440px-PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PolishedAmberMuseumAmberSanCris03.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Display of polished amber at the Museum of Amber
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       One of the main minerals of the state is
       <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">
        amber
       </a>
       , much of which is 25 million years old, with quality comparable to that found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">
        Dominican Republic
       </a>
       . Chiapan amber has a number of unique qualities, including much that is clear all the way through and some with fossilized insects and plants. Most Chiapan amber is worked into jewelry including
       <a href="/wiki/Pendant" title="Pendant">
        pendants
       </a>
       , rings and necklaces. Colors vary from white to yellow/orange to a deep red, but there are also green and pink tones as well. Since pre-Hispanic times, native peoples have believed amber to have healing and protective qualities. The largest amber mine is in Simojovel, a small village 130 km from Tuxtla Gutiérrez, which produces 95% of Chiapas' amber. Other mines are found in Huitiupán, Totolapa, El Bosque, Pueblo Nuevo Solistahuacán, Pantelhó and San Andrés Duraznal. According to the Museum of Amber in San Cristóbal, almost 300 kg of amber is extracted per month from the state. Prices vary depending on quality and color.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones43_130-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones43-130">
         [130]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The major center for ceramics in the state is the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Amatenango_del_Valle" title="Amatenango del Valle">
        Amatenango del Valle
       </a>
       , with its barro blanco (white clay) pottery.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones44_131-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones44-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most traditional ceramic in Amatenango and Aguacatenango is a type of large jar called a
       <a href="/wiki/Cantaro" title="Cantaro">
        cantaro
       </a>
       used to transport water and other liquids.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artepopdes_118-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-artepopdes-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many pieces created from this clay are ornamental as well as traditional pieces for everyday use such as comals, dishes, storage containers and flowerpots. All pieces here are made by hand using techniques that go back centuries. Other communities that produce ceramics include
       <a href="/wiki/Chiapa_de_Corzo,_Chiapas" title="Chiapa de Corzo, Chiapas">
        Chiapa de Corzo
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tonal%C3%A1,_Chiapas" title="Tonalá, Chiapas">
        Tonalá
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ocuilpa&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ocuilpa (page does not exist)">
        Ocuilpa
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Suchiapa" title="Suchiapa">
        Suchiapa
       </a>
       and San Cristóbal de las Casas.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones44_131-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones44-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
