       Wood crafts in the state center on furniture, brightly painted sculptures and toys. The Tzotzils of San Juan de Chamula are known for their sculptures as well as for their sturdy furniture. Sculptures are made from woods such as
       <a href="/wiki/Cedar_wood" title="Cedar wood">
        cedar
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Mahogany" title="Mahogany">
        mahogany
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muntingia_calabura" title="Muntingia calabura">
        strawberry tree
       </a>
       . Another town noted for their sculptures is
       <a href="/wiki/Tecpat%C3%A1n" title="Tecpatán">
        Tecpatán
       </a>
       . The making
       <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">
        lacquer
       </a>
       to use in the decoration of wooden and other items goes back to the colonial period. The best-known area for this type of work, called "laca" is Chiapa de Corzo, which has a museum dedicated to it. One reason this type of decoration became popular in the state was that it protected items from the constant humidity of the climate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones44_131-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones44-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Much of the laca in Chiapa de Corzo is made in the traditional way with natural pigments and sands to cover gourds, dipping spoons, chests, niches and furniture. It is also used to create the Parachicos masks.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artepopdes_118-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-artepopdes-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Mexican_handcrafted_toys" title="Traditional Mexican handcrafted toys">
        Traditional Mexican toys
       </a>
       , which have all but disappeared in the rest of Mexico, are still readily found here and include the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Cajita_de_la_serpiente&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Cajita de la serpiente (page does not exist)">
        cajita de la serpiente
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yo_yos" title="Yo yos">
        yo yos
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ball_in_cup" title="Ball in cup">
        ball in cup
       </a>
       and more. Other wooden items include masks, cooking utensils, and tools.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones44_131-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones44-131">
         [131]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One famous toy is the "muñecos zapatistas" (Zapatista dolls), which are based on the revolutionary group that emerged in the 1990s.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-artepopdes_118-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-artepopdes-118">
         [118]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span id="Tourism_and_general_commerce.2Fservices">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Tourism_and_general_commerce/services">
        Tourism and general commerce/services
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Tourism and general commerce/services">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG/220px-LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG/330px-LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG/440px-LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LanchaBoatFronteraCorozal02.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Boats at the docks of Frontera Corozal, which mostly serves the nearby Yaxchilan archeological site
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ninety four percent of the state's commercial outlets are small retail stores with about 6% wholesalers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There are 111
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_fixed_markets_in_Mexico" title="Traditional fixed markets in Mexico">
        municipal markets
       </a>
       , 55
       <a href="/wiki/Tianguis" title="Tianguis">
        tianguis
       </a>
       , three wholesale food markets and 173 large vendors of staple products.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_125-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-infraes-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The service sector is the most important to the economy, with mostly commerce, warehousing and tourism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Tourism brings large numbers of visitors to the state each year.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most of Chiapas' tourism is based on its culture, colonial cities and ecology. The state has a total of 491 ranked hotels with 12,122 rooms. There are also 780 other establishments catering primarily to tourism, such as services and restaurants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3144" data-file-width="4168" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg/220px-Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg/330px-Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg/440px-Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Misol-H%C3%A1_Waterfall.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Misol-Há Waterfall
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       There are three main tourist routes: the Maya Route, the Colonial Route and the Coffee Route. The Maya Route runs along the border with
       <a href="/wiki/Guatemala" title="Guatemala">
        Guatemala
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_Jungle" title="Lacandon Jungle">
        Lacandon Jungle
       </a>
       and includes the sites of
       <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">
        Palenque
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bonampak" title="Bonampak">
        Bonampak
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Yaxchilan" title="Yaxchilan">
        Yaxchilan
       </a>
       along with the natural attractions of
       <a href="/wiki/Agua_Azul" title="Agua Azul">
        Agua Azul Waterfalls
       </a>
       , Misol-Há Waterfall, and the Catazajá Lake.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Palenque is the most important of these sites, and one of the most important tourist destinations in the state. Yaxchilan was a Mayan city along the
       <a href="/wiki/Usumacinta_River" title="Usumacinta River">
        Usumacinta River
       </a>
       . It developed between 350 and 810 CE. Bonampak is known for its well preserved murals. These Mayan sites have made the state an attraction for international tourism.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisschmal_23-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisschmal-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zonasarq_88-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-zonasarq-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These sites contain a large number of structures, most of which date back thousands of years, especially to the sixth century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zonasarq_88-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-zonasarq-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones12_132-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones12-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition to the sites on the Mayan Route, there are others within the state away from the border such as
       <a href="/wiki/Tonin%C3%A1" title="Toniná">
        Toniná
       </a>
       , near the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Ocosingo" title="Ocosingo">
        Ocosingo
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zonasarq_88-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-zonasarq-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG/220px-RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG/330px-RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG/440px-RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RealdeGuadalupeEastSanCris.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Real de Guadalupe street in San Cristobal de las Casas
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The Colonial Route is mostly in the central highlands with a significant number of churches, monasteries and other structures from the colonial period along with some from the 19th century and even into the early 20th.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones12_132-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones12-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The most important city on this route is
       <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_las_Casas" title="San Cristóbal de las Casas">
        San Cristóbal de las Casas
       </a>
       , located in the Los Altos region in the Jovel Valley. The historic center of the city is filled with tiled roofs, patios with flowers, balconies,
       <a href="/wiki/Baroque_architecture" title="Baroque architecture">
        Baroque
       </a>
       facades along with
       <a href="/wiki/Neoclassical_architecture" title="Neoclassical architecture">
        Neoclassical
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Moorish_architecture" title="Moorish architecture">
        Moorish
       </a>
       designs. It is centered on a main plaza surrounded by the cathedral, the municipal palace, the Portales commercial area and the San Nicolás church. In addition, it has museums dedicated to the state’s indigenous cultures, one to amber and one to jade, both of which have been mined in the state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ciudadescol_133-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ciudadescol-133">
         [133]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Other attractions along this route include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comit%C3%A1n_de_Dom%C3%ADnguez" title="Comitán de Domínguez">
        Comitán de Domínguez
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Chiapa_de_Corzo_(Mesoamerican_site)" title="Chiapa de Corzo (Mesoamerican site)">
        Chiapa de Corzo
       </a>
       , along with small indigenous communities such as
       <a href="/wiki/Chamula" title="Chamula">
        San Juan Chamula
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones12_132-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones12-132">
         [132]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The state capital of
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
        Tuxtla Gutiérrez
       </a>
       does not have many colonial era structures left, but it lies near the area's most famous natural attraction of the
       <a href="/wiki/Sumidero_Canyon" title="Sumidero Canyon">
        Sumidero Canyon
       </a>
       . This canyon is popular with tourists who take boat tours into it on the
       <a href="/wiki/Grijalva_River" title="Grijalva River">
        Grijalva River
       </a>
       to see such features such as caves (La Cueva del Hombre, La Cueva del Silencio) and the Christmas Tree, which is a rock and plant formation on the side of one of the canyon walls created by a seasonal waterfall.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aventuragob_93-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-aventuragob-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atraccultur_134-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-atraccultur-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Coffee Route begins in
       <a href="/wiki/Tapachula" title="Tapachula">
        Tapachula
       </a>
       and follows a mountainous road into the Suconusco regopm. The route passes through
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_Chiapas" title="Port of Chiapas">
        Puerto Chiapas
       </a>
       , a port with modern infrastructure for shipping exports and receiving international cruises.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abianco_83-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-abianco-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The route visits a number of coffee plantations, such as Hamburgo, Chiripa, Violetas, Santa Rita, Lindavista, Perú-París, San Antonio Chicarras and Rancho Alegre. These haciendas provide visitors with the opportunity to see how coffee is grown and initially processed on these farms. They also offer a number of ecotourism activities such as mountain climbing, rafting, rappelling and mountain biking. There are also tours into the jungle vegetation and the
       <a href="/wiki/Volc%C3%A1n_Tacan%C3%A1" title="Volcán Tacaná">
        Tacaná Volcano
       </a>
       . In addition to coffee, the region also produces most of Chiapas’ soybeans, bananas and cacao.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atoussaint_89-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-atoussaint-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The state has a large number of ecological attractions most of which are connected to water. The main beaches on the coastline include
       <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Arista" title="Puerto Arista">
        Puerto Arista
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Boca_del_Cielo" title="Boca del Cielo">
        Boca del Cielo
       </a>
       , Playa Linda, Playa Aventuras, Playa Azul and Santa Brigida.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-acteconom_123-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-acteconom-123">
         [123]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-solplaya_135-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-solplaya-135">
         [135]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Others are based on the state's lakes and rivers. Laguna Verde is a lake in the Coapilla municipality. The lake is generally green but its tones constantly change through the day depending on how the sun strikes it. In the early morning and evening hours there can also be blue and ochre tones as well. The El Chiflón Waterfall is part of an ecotourism center located in a valley with reeds, sugarcane, mountains and rainforest. It is formed by the San Vicente River and has pools of water at the bottom popular for swimming. The Las Nubes Ecotourism center is located in the Las Margaritas municipality near the Guatemalan border. The area features a number of turquoise blue waterfalls with bridges and lookout points set up to see them up close.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aventuragob_93-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-aventuragob-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Still others are based on conservation, local culture and other features. The Las Guacamayas Ecotourism Center is located in the
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_Jungle" title="Lacandon Jungle">
        Lacandon Jungle
       </a>
       on the edge of the Montes Azules reserve. It is centered on the conservation of the red
       <a href="/wiki/Macaw" title="Macaw">
        macaw
       </a>
       , which is in danger of extinction. The Tziscao Ecotourism Center is centered on a lake with various tones. It is located inside the
       <a href="/wiki/Lagunas_de_Montebello_National_Park" title="Lagunas de Montebello National Park">
        Lagunas de Montebello National Park
       </a>
       , with kayaking, mountain biking and archery.
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lacanj%C3%A1_Chansayab&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lacanjá Chansayab (page does not exist)">
        Lacanjá Chansayab
       </a>
       is located in the interior of the Lacandon Jungle and a major Lacandon people community. It has some activities associated with ecotourism such as mountain biking, hiking and cabins. The Grutas de Rancho Nuevo Ecotourism Center is centered on a set of caves in which appear capricious forms of stalagmite and stalactites. There is also horseback riding as well.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aventuragob_93-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-aventuragob-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">
        Culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">
        Architecture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Architecture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Architecture in the state begins with the archeological sites of the Mayans and other groups who established color schemes and other details that echo in later structures. After the Spanish subdued the area, the building of Spanish style cities began, especially in the highland areas.
      </p>
      <p>
       Many of the colonial-era buildings are related to
       <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">
        Dominicans
       </a>
       who came from
       <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">
        Seville
       </a>
       . This Spanish city had much
       <a href="/wiki/Moorish_architecture" title="Moorish architecture">
        Arabic influence in its architecture
       </a>
       , and this was incorporated into the colonial architecture of Chiapas, especially in structures dating from the 16th to 18th centuries. However, there are a number of architectural styles and influences present in Chiapas colonial structures, including colors and patterns from
       <a href="/wiki/Oaxaca" title="Oaxaca">
        Oaxaca
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">
        Central America
       </a>
       along with indigenous ones from Chiapas.
      </p>
      <p>
       The main colonial structures are the cathedral and Santo Domingo church of San Cristóbal, the Santo Domingo monastery and La Pila in Chiapa de Corzo. The San Cristóbal cathedral has a Baroque facade that was begun in the 16th century but by the time it was finished in the 17th, it had a mix of Spanish, Arabic, and indigenous influences.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones13_136-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones13-136">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is one of the most elaborately decorated in Mexico.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones14_137-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones14-137">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The churches and former monasteries of Santo Domingo, La Merced and San Francisco have ornamentation similar to that of the cathedral. The main structures in Chiapa de Corzo are the Santo Domingo monastery and the La Pila fountain. Santo Domingo has indigenous decorative details such as double headed eagles as well as a statue of the founding monk. In San Cristóbal, the Diego de Mazariegos house has a
       <a href="/wiki/Plateresque" title="Plateresque">
        Plateresque
       </a>
       facade, while that of Francisco de Montejo, built later in the 18th century has a mix of Baroque and Neoclassical.
       <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">
        Art Deco
       </a>
       structures can be found in San Cristóbal and Tapachula in public buildings as well as a number of rural coffee plantations from the
       <a href="/wiki/Porfirio_D%C3%ADaz" title="Porfirio Díaz">
        Porfirio Díaz
       </a>
       era.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones14_137-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones14-137">
         [137]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_literature">
        Art and literature
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Art and literature">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Art in Chiapas is based on the use of color and has strong indigenous influence. This dates back to cave paintings such as those found in
       <a href="/wiki/Sima_de_las_Cotorras" title="Sima de las Cotorras">
        Sima de las Cotorras
       </a>
       near
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
        Tuxtla Gutiérrez
       </a>
       and the caverns of Rancho Nuevo where human remains and offerings were also found. The best-known pre-Hispanic artwork is the Maya murals of
       <a href="/wiki/Bonampak" title="Bonampak">
        Bonampak
       </a>
       , which are the only
       <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">
        Mesoamerican
       </a>
       murals to have been preserved for over 1500 years. In general, Mayan artwork stands out for its precise depiction of faces and its narrative form. Indigenous forms derive from this background and continue into the colonial period with the use of indigenous color schemes in churches and modern structures such as the municipal palace in
       <a href="/wiki/Tapachula" title="Tapachula">
        Tapachula
       </a>
       . Since the colonial period, the state has produced a large number of painters and sculptors. Noted 20th-century artists include
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=L%C3%A1zaro_G%C3%B3mez&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lázaro Gómez (page does not exist)">
        Lázaro Gómez
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ramiro_Jim%C3%A9nez_Chac%C3%B3n&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ramiro Jiménez Chacón (page does not exist)">
        Ramiro Jiménez Chacón
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=H%C3%A9ctor_Ventura_Cruz&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Héctor Ventura Cruz (page does not exist)">
        Héctor Ventura Cruz
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=M%C3%A1ximo_Prado_Pozo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Máximo Prado Pozo (page does not exist)">
        Máximo Prado Pozo
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gabriel_Gallegos_Ramos&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gabriel Gallegos Ramos (page does not exist)">
        Gabriel Gallegos Ramos
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1416_138-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones1416-138">
         [138]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The two best-known poets from the state include
       <a href="/wiki/Jaime_Sabines" title="Jaime Sabines">
        Jaime Sabines
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Rosario_Castellanos" title="Rosario Castellanos">
        Rosario Castellanos
       </a>
       , both from prominent Chiapan families. The first was a merchant and diplomat and the second was a teacher, diplomat, theatre director and the director of the Instituto Nacional Indigenista.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones16_139-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones16-139">
         [139]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Jaime Sabines is widely regarded as Mexico’s most influential contemporary poet. His work celebrates everyday people in common settings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Music">
        Music
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Music">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The most important instrument in the state is the
       <a href="/wiki/Marimba" title="Marimba">
        marimba
       </a>
       . In the pre Hispanic period, indigenous peoples had already been producing music with wooden instruments. The marimba was introduced by African slaves brought to Chiapas by the Spanish. However, it achieved its widespread popularity in the early 20th century due to the formation of the Cuarteto Marimbistico de los Hermanos Gómez in 1918, who popularized the instrument and the popular music they play not only in Chiapas but in various parts of Mexico and into the United States. Along with Cuban Juan Arozamena, they composed the piece "Las chiapanecas" considered to be the unofficial anthem of the state. In the 1940s, they were also featured in a number of Mexican films.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones18_108-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones18-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Marimbas are constructed in Venustiano Carranza, Chiapas de Corzo and Tuxtla Gutiérrez.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atraccultur_134-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-atraccultur-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cuisine">
        Cuisine
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Cuisine">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_Chiapas" title="Cuisine of Chiapas">
        cuisine of Chiapas
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Like the rest of
       <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">
        Mesoamerica
       </a>
       , the basic diet has been based on corn and Chiapas cooking retains strong indigenous influence. One important ingredient is
       <a href="/wiki/Crotalaria" title="Crotalaria">
        chipilin
       </a>
       , a fragrant and strongly flavored herb and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoja_santa" title="Hoja santa">
        hoja santa
       </a>
       , the large anise-scented leaves used in much of southern Mexican cuisine.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Chiapan dishes do not incorporate many chili peppers as part of their dishes. Rather, chili peppers are most often found in the condiments. One reason for that is that a local chili pepper, called the simojovel, is far too hot to use except very sparingly. Chiapan cuisine tends to rely more on slightly sweet seasonings in their main dishes such as cinnamon,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plantain_(cooking)" title="Plantain (cooking)">
        plantains
       </a>
       , prunes and pineapple are often found in meat and poultry dishes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Tamale" title="Tamale">
        Tamales
       </a>
       are a major part of the diet and often include chipilín mixed into the dough and hoja santa, within the tamale itself or used to wrap it. One tamale native to the state is the "picte", a fresh sweet corn tamale.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tamales juacanes are filled with a mixture of black beans, dried shrimp, and pumpkin seeds.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Meats are centered on the European introduced beef, pork and chicken as many native game animals are in danger of extinction. Meat dishes are frequently accompanied by vegetables such as squash,
       <a href="/wiki/Chayote" title="Chayote">
        chayote
       </a>
       and carrots. Black beans are the favored type. Beef is favored, especially a thin cut called
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tasajo&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tasajo (page does not exist)">
        tasajo
       </a>
       usually served in a sauce.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Pepita con tasajo is a common dish at festivals especially in Chiapa de Corzo. It consists of a squash seed based sauced over reconstituted and shredded dried beef. As a cattle raising area, beef dishes in Palenque are particularly good. Pux-Xaxé is a stew with beef organ meats and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mole_(sauce)" title="Mole (sauce)">
        mole sauce
       </a>
       made with tomato, chili bolita and corn flour.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tzispolá is a beef broth with chunks of meat, chickpeas, cabbage and various types of chili peppers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Pork dishes include cochito, which is pork in an
       <a href="/wiki/Adobo" title="Adobo">
        adobo
       </a>
       sauce. In Chiapa de Corzo, their version is cochito horneado, which is a roast suckling pig flavored with adobo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Seafood is a strong component in many dishes along the coast.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Turula is dried shrimp with tomatoes. Sausages, ham and other cold cuts are most often made and consumed in the highlands.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:177px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TaxcalatePalenque.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3872" data-file-width="2592" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/TaxcalatePalenque.JPG/175px-TaxcalatePalenque.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/TaxcalatePalenque.JPG/263px-TaxcalatePalenque.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/TaxcalatePalenque.JPG/350px-TaxcalatePalenque.JPG 2x" width="175"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TaxcalatePalenque.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Drink called taxcalate
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       In addition to meat dishes, there is chirmol, a cooked tomato sauced flavored with chili pepper, onion and cilantro and zats, butterfly caterpillars from the Altos de Chiapas that are boiled in salted water, then sautéed in
       <a href="/wiki/Lard" title="Lard">
        lard
       </a>
       and eaten with tortillas, limes, and green chili pepper.
      </p>
      <p>
       Sopa de pan consists of layers of bread and vegetables covered with a broth seasoned with saffron and other flavorings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A Comitán speciality is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hearts_of_palm" title="Hearts of palm">
        hearts of palm
       </a>
       salad in
       <a href="/wiki/Vinaigrette" title="Vinaigrette">
        vinaigrette
       </a>
       and Palenque is known for many versions of fried plaintains, including filled with black beans or cheese.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Cheese making is important, especially in the municipalities of Ocosingo, Rayon and Pijijiapan. Ocosingo has its own self-named variety, which is shipped to restaurants and gourmet shops in various parts of the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Regional sweets include crystallized fruit, coconut candies, flan and
       <a href="/wiki/Compote" title="Compote">
        compotes
       </a>
       . San Cristobal is noted for its sweets, as well as chocolates, coffee and baked goods.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While Chiapas is known for good coffee, there are a number of other local beverages. The oldest is
       <a href="/wiki/Pozol" title="Pozol">
        pozol
       </a>
       , originally the name for a fermented corn dough. This dough has its origins in the pre Hispanic period. To make the beverage, the dough is dissolved in water and usually flavored with cocoa and sugar, but sometimes it is left to ferment further. It is then served very cold with lots of ice.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chicuisine_141-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-chicuisine-141">
         [141]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Taxcalate is a drink made from a powder of toasted corn,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achiote" title="Achiote">
        achiote
       </a>
       , cinnamon and sugar prepared with milk or water. Pumbo is a beverage made with pineapple, club soda, vodka, sugar syrup and lots of ice. Posh is a drink distilled from sugar cane.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bsantos_140-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-bsantos-140">
         [140]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">
        Religion
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Religion">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Like in the rest of Mexico, Christianity was imposed on the native population by the Spanish
       <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">
        conquistadors
       </a>
       . Catholic beliefs were mixed with indigenous ones to form what is now called "traditionalist" Catholic belief.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-relmix_142-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-relmix-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Diocese_of_San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_Las_Casas" title="Roman Catholic Diocese of San Cristóbal de Las Casas">
        Diocese of Chiapas
       </a>
       comprises almost the entire state, and centered on San Cristobal de las Casas. It was founded in 1538 by
       <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">
        Pope Paul III
       </a>
       to evangelize the area with its most famous bishop of that time
       <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_de_las_Casas" title="Bartolomé de las Casas">
        Bartolomé de las Casas
       </a>
       . Evangelization focused on grouping indigenous peoples into communities centered on a church. This bishop not only had these people evangelized in their own language, he worked to introduce many of the crafts still practiced today.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cathenc_143-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-cathenc-143">
         [143]
        </a>
       </sup>
       While still a majority, only sixty-eight percent of Chiapas residents profess the Catholic faith as of 2010, compared to 83% of the rest of the country.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegidiv_112-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-inegidiv-112">
         [112]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Many indigenous people mix Christianity with Indian beliefs. One particular area where this is strong is the central highlands in small communities such as San Juan Chamula. In one church in San Cristobal, Mayan rites including the sacrifice of animals is permitted inside the church to ask for good health or to "ward off the evil eye."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-relmix_142-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-relmix-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Starting in the 1970s, there has been a shift away from traditional Catholic affiliation to Protestant, Evangelical and other Christian denominations.
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presbyterians" title="Presbyterians">
        Presbyterians
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pentecostals" title="Pentecostals">
        Pentecostals
       </a>
       attracted a large number of converts, with percentages of Protestants in the state rising from five percent in 1970 to twenty-one percent in 2000. This shift has had a political component as well, with those making the switch tending to identify across ethnic boundaries, especially across indigenous ethnic boundaries and being against the traditional power structure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mhidalgo108_52-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-mhidalgo108-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/National_Presbyterian_Church_in_Mexico" title="National Presbyterian Church in Mexico">
        National Presbyterian Church in Mexico
       </a>
       is particularly strong in Chiapas, the state can be described as one of the strongholds of the denomination.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
        <a href="#cite_note-144">
         [144]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       To counter this, the Diocese of Chiapas began to actively re-evangelize among the indigenous populations, and working on their behalf politically as well, following an ideology called
       <a href="/wiki/Liberation_theology" title="Liberation theology">
        liberation theology
       </a>
       . Those attracted by this movement call themselves "Word of God" Catholics and identify directly with the Diocese, rather than with local Catholic authorities. Both Protestants and Word of God Catholics tend to oppose traditional cacique leadership and often worked to prohibit the sale of alcohol. The latter had the effect of attracting many women to both movements.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The growing number of Protestants, Evangelicals and Word of God Catholics challenging traditional authority has caused religious strife in a number of indigenous communities. Tensions have been strong, at times, especially in rural areas such as San Juan Chamula. Tension among the groups reached its peak in the 1990s with a large number of people injured during open clashes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-relmix_142-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-relmix-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In the 1970s, caciques began to expel dissidents from their communities for challenging their power, initially with the use of violence. By 2000, more than 20,000 people had been displaced, but state and federal authorities did not act to stop the expulsions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kovic_63-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-kovic-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, the situation has quieted but the tension remains, especially in very isolated communities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-relmix_142-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-relmix-142">
         [142]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Archeology">
        Archeology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Archeology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="1586" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG/220px-TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG/330px-TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG/440px-TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TiltepecSculptureMusRegTuxtla2.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Olmec style stone sculpture from Tiltepec at the Regional Museum of Chiapas
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The earliest population of Chiapas was in the coastal
       <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">
        Soconusco
       </a>
       region, where the
       <a href="/wiki/Chantuto_Archaeological_Site" title="Chantuto Archaeological Site">
        Chantuto peoples
       </a>
       appeared, going back to 5500 BC. This was the oldest Mesoamerican culture discovered to date.
      </p>
      <p>
       The largest and best-known archeological sites in Chiapas belong to the Mayan civilization. Apart from a few works by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Franciscan" title="Franciscan">
        Franciscan
       </a>
       friars, knowledge of Maya civilisation largely disappeared after the
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_conquest_of_the_Aztec_Empire" title="Spanish conquest of the Aztec Empire">
        Spanish Conquest
       </a>
       . In the mid-19th century,
       <a href="/wiki/John_Lloyd_Stephens" title="John Lloyd Stephens">
        John Lloyd Stephens
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Catherwood" title="Frederick Catherwood">
        Frederick Catherwood
       </a>
       traveled though the sites in Chiapas and other Mayan areas and published their writings and illustrations. This led to serious work on the culture including the deciphering of its hieroglyphic writing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamnett32_145-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamnett32-145">
         [145]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In Chiapas, principal Mayan sites include
       <a href="/wiki/Palenque" title="Palenque">
        Palenque
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tonin%C3%A1" title="Toniná">
        Toniná
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Bonampak" title="Bonampak">
        Bonampak
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Chinkoltic&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Chinkoltic (page does not exist)">
        Chinkoltic
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tenam_Puentes&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tenam Puentes (page does not exist)">
        Tenam Puentes
       </a>
       , all or near in the
       <a href="/wiki/Lacandon_Jungle" title="Lacandon Jungle">
        Lacandon Jungle
       </a>
       . They are technically more advanced than earlier
       <a href="/wiki/Olmec" title="Olmec">
        Olmec
       </a>
       sites, which can best be seen in the detailed sculpting and novel construction techniques, including structures of four stories in height. Mayan sites are not only noted for large numbers of structures, but also for glyphs, other inscriptions, and artwork that has provided a relatively complete history of many of the sites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones13_136-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones13-136">
         [136]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Palenque is the most important Mayan and archeological site. Though much smaller than the huge sites at
       <a href="/wiki/Tikal" title="Tikal">
        Tikal
       </a>
       or
       <a href="/wiki/Cop%C3%A1n" title="Copán">
        Copán
       </a>
       , Palenque contains some of the finest architecture, sculpture and
       <a href="/wiki/Stucco" title="Stucco">
        stucco
       </a>
       reliefs the Mayans ever produced.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The history of the Palenque site begins in 431 with its height under Pakal I (615-683), Chan-Bahlum II (684-702) and Kan-Xul who reigned between 702 and 721. However, the power of Palenque would be lost by the end of the century. Pakal’s tomb was not discovered inside the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Inscriptions" title="Temple of Inscriptions">
        Temple of Inscriptions
       </a>
       until 1949.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamnett36_146-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamnett36-146">
         [146]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Today, Palenque is a
       <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">
        World Heritage Site
       </a>
       and one of the best-known sites in Mexico.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zonasarq_88-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-zonasarq-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Yaxchilan flourished in the 8th and 9th centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamnett34_147-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Hamnett34-147">
         [147]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The site contains impressive ruins, with palaces and temples bordering a large plaza upon a terrace above the Usumacinta River.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ST435_148-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ST435-148">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The architectural remains extend across the higher terraces and the hills to the south of the river, overlooking both the river itself and the lowlands beyond.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ST435_148-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ST435-148">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Yaxchilan is known for the large quantity of excellent sculpture at the site, such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Monolith#Monumental_monoliths" title="Monolith">
        monolithic
       </a>
       carved
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stela" title="Stela">
        stelae
       </a>
       and the narrative stone
       <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">
        reliefs
       </a>
       carved on
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lintel_(architecture)" title="Lintel (architecture)">
        lintels
       </a>
       spanning the temple doorways.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ST435_148-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-ST435-148">
         [148]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MartinGrube_149-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MartinGrube-149">
         [149]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Over 120 inscriptions have been identified on the various monuments from the site.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_150-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-150">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The major groups are the Central Acropolis, the West Acropolis and the South Acropolis. The South Acropolis occupies the highest part of the site.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelly_150-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kelly-150">
         [150]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The site is aligned with relation to the Usumacinta River, at times causing unconventional orientation of the major structures, such as the two
       <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerican_ballcourt" title="Mesoamerican ballcourt">
        ballcourts
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scarborough_151-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Scarborough-151">
         [151]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The city of Bonampak features some of the finest remaining Maya murals. The realistically rendered paintings depict human sacrifices, musicians and scenes of the royal court.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-historycom_22-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-historycom-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In fact the name means “painted murals.” It is centered on a large plaza and has a stairway that leads to the Acropolis. There are also a number of notable steles.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zonasarq_88-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-zonasarq-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Toniná is near the city of
       <a href="/wiki/Ocosingo" title="Ocosingo">
        Ocosingo
       </a>
       with its main features being the Casa de Piedra (House of Stone) and Acropolis. The latter is a series of seven platforms with various temples and steles. This site was a ceremonial center that flourished between 600 and 900 CE.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zonasarq_88-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-zonasarq-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Mayan_cultures">
        Pre-Mayan cultures
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Pre-Mayan cultures">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       While the Mayan sites are the best-known, there are a number of other important sites in the state, including many older than the Maya civilization.
      </p>
      <p>
       The oldest sites are in the coastal
       <a href="/wiki/Soconusco" title="Soconusco">
        Soconusco
       </a>
       region. This includes the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mokaya_culture" title="Mokaya culture">
        Mokaya culture
       </a>
       , the oldest ceramic culture of Mesoamerica. Later,
       <a href="/wiki/Paso_de_la_Amada" title="Paso de la Amada">
        Paso de la Amada
       </a>
       became important. Many of these sites are in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazatan,_Chiapas" title="Mazatan, Chiapas">
        Mazatan, Chiapas
       </a>
       area.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Izapa" title="Izapa">
        Izapa
       </a>
       became an important pre-Mayan site as well.
      </p>
      <p>
       There are also other ancient sites including
       <a href="/wiki/Tapachula" title="Tapachula">
        Tapachula
       </a>
       and
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Tepcat%C3%A1n&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Tepcatán (page does not exist)">
        Tepcatán
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Pijijiapan" title="Pijijiapan">
        Pijijiapan
       </a>
       . These sites contain numerous embankments and foundations that once lay beneath pyramids and other buildings. Some of these buildings have disappeared and others have been covered by jungle for about 3,000 years, unexplored.
      </p>
      <p>
       Pijijiapan and Izapa are on the Pacific coast and were the most important pre Hispanic cities for about 1,000 years, as the most important commercial centers between the
       <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Plateau" title="Mexican Plateau">
        Mexican Plateau
       </a>
       and Central America.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones1213_152-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones1213-152">
         [152]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Sima_de_las_Cotorras" title="Sima de las Cotorras">
        Sima de las Cotorras
       </a>
       is a sinkhole 140 meters deep with a diameter of 160 meters in the municipality of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocozocoautla" title="Ocozocoautla">
        Ocozocoautla
       </a>
       . It contains ancient cave paintings depicting warriors, animals and more. It is best known as a breeding area for parrots, thousands of which leave the area at once at dawn and return at dusk.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aventuragob_93-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-aventuragob-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
        <a href="#cite_note-153">
         [153]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The state as its Museo Regional de Antropologia e Historia located in Tuxtla Gutiérrez focusing on the pre Hispanic peoples of the state with a room dedicated to its history from the colonial period.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atraccultur_134-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-atraccultur-134">
         [134]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
        Education
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Education">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The average number of years of schooling is 6.7, which is the beginning of middle school, compared to the Mexico average of 8.6. 16.5% have no schooling at all, 59.6% have only primary school/secondary school, 13.7% finish high school or technical school and 9.8% go to university. Eighteen out of every 100 people 15 years or older cannot read or write, compared to 7/100 nationally.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inegiedu_154-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-inegiedu-154">
         [154]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Most of Chiapas’ illiterate population are indigenous women, who are often prevented from going to school. School absenteeism and dropout rates are highest among indigenous girls.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-adultwomen_71-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-adultwomen-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are an estimated 1.4 million students in the state from preschool on up. The state has about 61,000 teachers and just over 17,000 centers of educations. Preschool and primary schools are divided into modalities called general, indigenous, private and community educations sponsored by CONAFE. Middle school is divided into technical, telesecundaria (distance education) and classes for working adults. About 98% of the student population of the state is in state schools. Higher levels of education include "professional medio" (vocational training), general high school and technology-focused high school. At this level, 89% of students are in public schools. There are 105 universities and similar institutions with 58 public and 47 private serving over 60,500 students.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_125-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-infraes-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The state university is the
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Universidad_Aut%C3%B3noma_de_Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Universidad Autónoma de Chiapas (page does not exist)">
        Universidad Autónoma de Chiapas
       </a>
       (UNACH). It was begun when an organization to establish a state level institution was formed in 1965, with the university itself opening its doors ten years later in 1975.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rincones35_19-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-rincones35-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The university project was partially supported by
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       in Mexico. It integrated older schools such as the Escuela de Derecho (Law School), which originated in 1679; the Escuela de Ingeniería Civil (School of Civil Engineering), founded in 1966; and the Escuela de Comercio y Administración, which was located in
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
        Tuxtla Gutiérrez
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hisuchiapas_155-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hisuchiapas-155">
         [155]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">
        Infrastructure
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Puertochis1.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Puertochis1.JPG/220px-Puertochis1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Puertochis1.JPG/330px-Puertochis1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Puertochis1.JPG/440px-Puertochis1.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Puertochis1.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         View of Puerto Chiapas
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TGZ_Airport.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/TGZ_Airport.jpg/250px-TGZ_Airport.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/TGZ_Airport.jpg/375px-TGZ_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/TGZ_Airport.jpg/500px-TGZ_Airport.jpg 2x" width="250"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TGZ_Airport.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%81ngel_Albino_Corzo_International_Airport" title="Ángel Albino Corzo International Airport">
          Ángel Albino Corzo International Airport
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The state has approximately 22,517 km of highway with 10,857 federally maintained and 11,660 maintained by the state. Almost all of these kilometers are paved. Major highways include the Las Choapas-Raudales-Ocozocoautla, which links the state to Oaxaca, Veracruz, Puebla and Mexico City. Major airports include Llano San Juan in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ocozocoautla" title="Ocozocoautla">
        Ocozocoautla
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Sarabia_National_Airport" title="Francisco Sarabia National Airport">
        Francisco Sarabia National Airport
       </a>
       (which was replaced by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%81ngel_Albino_Corzo_International_Airport" title="Ángel Albino Corzo International Airport">
        Ángel Albino Corzo International Airport
       </a>
       ) in
       <a href="/wiki/Tuxtla_Guti%C3%A9rrez" title="Tuxtla Gutiérrez">
        Tuxtla Gutiérrez
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coraz%C3%B3n_de_Mar%C3%ADa_Airport" title="Corazón de María Airport">
        Corazón de María Airport
       </a>
       (which closed in 2010) in
       <a href="/wiki/San_Crist%C3%B3bal_de_las_Casas" title="San Cristóbal de las Casas">
        San Cristóbal de las Casas
       </a>
       . These are used for domestic flights with the airports in Palenque and Tapachula providing international service into Guatemala. There are 22 other airfields in twelve other municipalities. Rail lines extend over 547.8 km. There are two major lines: one in the north of the state that links the center and southeast of the country, and the Costa Panamericana route, which runs from Oaxaca to the Guatemalan border.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_125-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-infraes-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There are thirty six AM radio stations and sixteen FM stations. There are thirty seven local television stations and sixty six repeaters.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_125-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-infraes-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Chiapas' main port is just outside the city of Tapachula called the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Port_of_Chiapas" title="Port of Chiapas">
        Puerto Chiapas
       </a>
       . It faces 3,361 meters of ocean, with 3,060 m2 of warehouse space. Next to it there is an industrial park that covers 2,340,000 m2.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-infraes_125-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-infraes-125">
         [125]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Puerto Chiapas has 60,000 m2 of area with a capacity to receive 1,800 containers as well as refrigerated containers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-exportacion_156-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-exportacion-156">
         [156]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The port serves the state of Chiapas and northern Guatemala.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hinterland_157-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hinterland-157">
         [157]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Puerto Chiapas serves to import and export products across the Pacific to Asia, the United States, Canada and
       <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">
        South America
       </a>
       . It also has connections with the
       <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">
        Panama Canal
       </a>
       . A marina serves yachts in transit. There is an international airport located eleven km away as well as a railroad terminal ending at the port proper.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-conectividad_158-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-conectividad-158">
         [158]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Over the past five years the port has grown with its newest addition being a terminal for cruise ships with tours to the Izapa site, the Coffee Route, the city of Tapachula, Pozuelos Lake and an Artesanal Chocolate Tour. Principal exports through the port include banana and banana trees, corn, fertilizer and tuna.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seconsolida_159-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-seconsolida-159">
         [159]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">
        Sports
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Sports">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The capital of the state has a professional football team called
       <a href="/wiki/Chiapas_F.C." title="Chiapas F.C.">
        Chiapas F.C.
       </a>
       that competes in the
       <a href="/wiki/Liga_MX" title="Liga MX">
        Liga MX
       </a>
       , located in Tuxtla Gutiérrez. This team made
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primera_Divisi%C3%B3n_de_M%C3%A9xico" title="Primera División de México">
        Mexico's first division
       </a>
       in 2002. The team changed its symbol and colors to orange and black in 2010. It participated in the
       <a href="/wiki/Copa_Libertadores" title="Copa Libertadores">
        Copa Libertadores de América
       </a>
       in 2011.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clubhis_160-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-clubhis-160">
         [160]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">
        See also
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Chiapas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: See also">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>

         </span>

           <img alt="icon" class="noviewer" data-file-height="348" data-file-width="368" height="28" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/North_America_368x348.png/30px-North_America_368x348.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/North_America_368x348.png/44px-North_America_368x348.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/North_America_368x348.png/59px-North_America_368x348.png 2x" width="30"/>
          </a>
         </span>
         <span style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle">
          <a href="/wiki/Portal:North_America" title="Portal:North America">
           North America portal
          </a>
         </span>
        </li>

       <span class="mw-editsection">

       <li>

       <li>

        , Vol. 10, no. 2 (Summer 1994): pp. 371–382
       </li>
       <li>

        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">

         978-0-521-61802-1
        </a>
       </li>
       <li>
        Hidalgo, Margarita G. (Editor).
        <i>
         Contributions to the Sociology of Language : Mexican Indigenous Languages at the Dawn of the Twenty-First Century

        <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
